\'INDEX\n\n.\n\nI-1\n\n..\n\nREFERENCES\n\nACRONYMS,\n\nPage\n\n..\n\n.........\n\n.\n..............\n\n...................................\n\n.J-1\n...\n\nK-1\n\nL\xc2\xad\nL1..\nM-1\n\nvi\n\nSECTION 1 \n\nINTRODUCTION \n\n\nThe ERTS Data Users Handbook was first\npublished in December of 1971 and was\nrevised periodically through December of\n1972 Anticipating the widespread popularity\nof this first major step in space remote sensing\nof earth\'s resources, every effort was made to\nget the handbook into investigator\'s and\npotential user\'s hands as quickly as possible.\nPublication of the document before the\nlaunch of Landsat 1 and its revision after only\nsix months of orbital operation has resulted in\nnumerous errors and inaccuracies throughout\nthe handbook. This total revision, the Landsat\nData Users Handbook, corrects those defects,\nreflects current users needs more accurately\n(especially in the area of computer compatible\ntapes) and incorporates as much information\nas possible about the third spacecraft in the\nLandsat series, Landsat-C\n\nand user\'s needs for information about Land\xc2\xad\nsat data products and how to acquire them\nThroughout the Landsat program\'s successful\nfive-year history, both the data products\nthemselves and the procedures for acquiring\nthem have changed considerably These\nchanges have also been included in this\nrevision\nN\' Irlt> di ...\nc \'\'\nAs in the original document, the main body of\nthe handbook provides information of interest\nto all users. Section 2 provides a concise\ndescription of the Landsat system. A new\nsection providing a brief description of some\nof the applications of Landsat data has been\nincluded as Section 3 Detailed descriptions of\ndata products are provided in Section 4, and\nthe new sources and procedures for obtaining\nLandsat data are described in Section 5.\nAppendices provide more detailed treatment\nof topics of interest to most investigators and\nusers to various degrees. Appendices C and\' D\nhave been completely rewritten to provide\nextensive detail on the MSS and computer\ncompatible tapes, respectively. They have\nbeen designed to satisfy an expressed need\namong users for detailed digital information\nthat is vital to their use of Landsat data in\ncomputer environments. Appendix F has also\nbeen completely rewritten to reflect the latest\norbital and image reference designation in\xc2\xad\nformation Recent and planned hardware and\nprocedural changes in the NASA Image Pro\xc2\xad\ncessing Facility (formerly the NASA Data\n\nThe intent of this revised handbook is-the\nsame as the original\' to satisfy investigator\'s\n\nProcessing Facility) are described in Appendix\nH. The other appendices have been revised to\nreflect the most current information available\n\nThe first satellite in the Earth Resources\nTechnology Satellite (ERTS) program was\nlaunched in July 1972 and designated ERTS\n1. It was followed into space by a second\nsatellite in January of 1975. At that time the\nprogram was redesignated as the Landsat\nprogram to emphasize its prime area of\ninterest, the resources of land masses. The two\nin-orbit satellites were accordingly renamed\nLandsat 1 and Landsat 2.\n\nr\n\nI-1 2\n\n\ntative measurments to be made of earth\xc2\xad\nsurface charcterrstics on a spectral, spatial,\nand temporal basis\n\nSECTION 2 \n\nLANDSAT SYSTEM DESCRIPTION \n\n2.1 LANDSAT MISSION\n\nThe overall Landsat 1 and 2 System and the\nplanned Landsat-C System are illustrated in\nFigure 2-1 The Observatory carries a payload\nof imaging multispectral sensors (MSS and\nRBV except for the RBV on Landsat-C, which\nis panchromatic), wideband video tape re\xc2\xad\ncorders, and the spaceborne portion of a Data\nCollection System (DCS). The spacecraft\n"housekeeping" telemetry, tracking, and corn\xc2\xad\nmand subsystems are compatible with stations\nfrom NASA\'s Space Tracking and Data Net\xc2\xad\nwork (STDN). Wideband payload video data\nare received at Fairbanks, Alaska; Goldstone,\nCalifornia, and the GSFC Network Test and\n\nThe mission of Landsat is to provide for\nthe repetitive acquisition of high resolution\nmultispectral data of the earth\'s surface on aglobal basis. Two sensor systems have been\nselected for this purpose- A four-channel\n(five-channel on Landsat-C) multispectral\nscanner (MSS) system and a three-camera\n(two-camera on Landsat-C) return beam\nvidicon (RBV) system- In addition, the\nObservatory is utilized as a relay system to\ngather data from remote, widely distributed,\nearth-based sensor platforms equipped by\nindividual investigators. The data acquired by\nthe total Landsat System thus permits quanti-\n\niiSENSING\n\nCOMMANfDIS\n\nA\n\nTRACKING \n\n\nPLATFOR \n\n\nBV&\nMS\n\nSA RIC\n\n\nCSD ATA,\nH"OUSEKEEPINGFFC\n\nIMGEA\n\nJ \n\n\nG\nTELEMETRY,\nTRACKIN\nBATA,PAYLOADVIDEO\nDATA\n\nSYSTEM\nDATAHANDLING\nGROUND\n\nFigr\n\nCENTER\n\n-FCIIAKD \n\n\nD-TCONTROL\n\nBACUPUS\n7""NS\n\nTRKG\n\nI R4ATONS\n\nFACILITY\n\nROC.ING\n\nPAYLOADVIDEOTAPES(MAILED\nFROMALASKAAN D\nGOLDSTONfE) DIRECTFROMHTTF)\nOR\n\nGSF\n\nAT ,\n\nGOLDSTONE\nCALIFORNIA\n\nFigure2-1 Overall LandsatSystem\n\n2-1\n\n\nD\n\nENTERS\n\nTraining Facility (NTTF) at Greenbelt, Maryland Payload data are also being received at\nnon-U.S, ground stations in western Canada,\nBrazil and Italy. Additional non-U.S ground\nstations are planned for eastern Canada, Iran,\nZaire and Chile.\n\nAttitude Measurement Sensor (AMS), oper\xc2\xad\nating over a narrow range of about \xc2\xb1 2\ndegrees, provides pitch and roll attitude data\naccurate to within 0.07 degree to aid in image\nlocation. Orbit adjustment capability is fur\xc2\xad\nnished by a monopropellant hydrazine subsys\xc2\xad\ntem employing one-pound force thrusters.\nThis system is used to remove launch vehicle\ninjection errors, and to provide periodic trim\nto maintain an orbit with an 18-day repetitive\ncycle\n\nThe Operations Control Center (OCC) is the\nfocal point of mission orbital operations. Here\nthe overall system isscheduled, spacecraft\ncommands are originated and orbital opera\xc2\xad\ntions are executed and evaluated DCS, telemetry, and command data transfer between the\nOCC and remote ground sites isaccomplished\nby NASA Communications (NASCOM) The\nNASA Image Processing Facility (IPF) accepts\npayload video data in the form of magnetic\ntapes received in real time at the NTTF\nStation via the OCC or by mail from Alaska\nand Goldstone. The IPF then performs the\nvideo-to-film conversion producing black and\nwhite images from individual spectral bands\nand color composites from several bands. The\nIPF includes astorage and retrieval system for\nall data acquired by the U.S. and provides for\ndelivery of data products and services to the\nprimary data centers and other government\norganizations. Together, the OCC and IPF\ncomprise the Landsat Ground Data Handling\nSystem (GDHS).\n\nPayload video data are transmitted to ground\nstations over two wideband S-band data links.\nTraveling wave tube amplifiers with com\xc2\xad\nmandable power output and shaped beam\nantennas are used in this subsystem to provide\nmaximum fidelity of the payload data at\nminimum power The two links are identi\xc2\xad\ncal, though operating at different frequencies,\nand are interchangeable. Each link is com\xc2\xad\npatible with data from either of the two imag\xc2\xad\ning sensors (the RBV and the MSS). Cross\xc2\xad\nstrapping and dual mode operation with a\nsingle amplifier is provided to assure system\n\nSOLAR\n\nSUYEM\n\nARRAY\n\n2.2 OBSERVATORY SYSTEM\nThe elements of the Landsat Observatory.\nSystem include the payload subsystems and\nthe various support subsystems comprising\n\n-.\n\nDEN..0\n\n\nthe spacecraft vehicle. It uses horizon scanners for pitch and\nroll control, and a gyrocompassing mode for\nyaw\n\norientation\n\nAn\n\nindependent\n\nANTENNtA\n\n\nC\nDATA OLLECTION\nATN\n\nSENSOR\n\nSCANNER\n\nf [i\nLTiPECTRAL\n"\n\nANTENNACUN BAMR\n\nCON\nVIOICON ERAS\n\nCAM\nDON8\nLANIDSAT\ntA4I\nLANDSAT72\n\n\n2ON\n\npassive\n\nFigure 2-2. Telemetry, tracking, and command\ncapability are fully compatible with the\n\nTHREE RBV\n\n-CAMERAS\nMOUNTEDIN\n\nSPACECRAFT\n\n\nSTDN system. Electrical power is generated\n\nby two independently driven solar arrays,\nwith storage provided by batteries for spacecraft eclipse periods and during launch.\n\n/I\n\nThe payload equipment is centrally packaged\n\nin a circular structure at the base of the spacecraft, providing close proximity between the\npayload sensors, their electronics, and\' wide\xc2\xad\nband communications equipment. users have demonstrated the\ncapability to monitor and inventory many\ndifferent resources within this discipline.\n\n3.1 INTRODUCTION\nThis section provides new and prospective\nusers of Landsat data with a broad overview of the data analysis methods and applications developed by Landsat investigators\nIt is intended to point out some of\nthose methods and applications that have\nproven successful in each of the various\ndisciplines, and to stimulate the imaginations\nof prospective data users to find new ways of\nextracting and using satellite remotely-sensed\ninformation.\nIt should be noted for those who intend to\nIt soul benote wo iten\nfo thse\n\ntooverall\n\nwork with digital data that computer compatible tapes produced from the original video\ntapes contain more radiometric and geometric\ninformation than tapes produced by the di-\n\n3.2 DISCIPLINE-ORIENTED\n\nCrop acreage inventory application studies\nhave identified the following techniques as\ncontributing to high crop identification and\narea measurement accuracies: preliminary\nstratification of the data, the use of temporal\n(multidate) data, unequal probability analysis\nand gray level histogram trimming\nStra\xc2\xad\ntification, generally done using photointer\xc2\xad\npretation, significantly reduces the number of\nclasses that must be considered during de\xc2\xad\ntailed analyses, thus reducing the time re\xc2\xad\nquired to do the classification and improving\nclassification accuracy (Draeger, Erb).\nTemporal analysis has been found to either\nimprove accuracy beyond that of single date\nanalysis or to make possible the earlier ach\xc2\xad\nievement of an equivalent accuracy level\n(Erb, \t Schaller). Unequal probability analysis\nassumes that not all species have an equal\nprobability of appearing in the area under\nstudy and makes final identification decisions\nusing prior knowledge of probability of oc\xc2\xad\ncurrence (VonSteen)\nGray level histogram\ntrimming, an interactive, individual-band gray\nlevel range adjustment technique, has been\nused very successfully for training signature\nrefinement (Dietrich). In addition to dem\xc2\xad\nonstrating the usefulness of these techniques,\ninvestigators studying crop inventory applica\xc2\xad\ntions have found that classification accur\xc2\xad\nacies using bands 5 and 7 only, are as high as\nwhen bands 4, 5, 6, and 7 are used for sig\xc2\xad\nnature development and classification.\n\nOVERVIEWS\n\nApplications of Landsat data have been redated to various "earth sciences" disciplines\nfrom the inception of the program. These disciplines are. Agriculture/Forestry/Range Resources, Land Use Survey and Mapping, Mneral Resources, Geological, Structural and\nLandform Survey; Water Resources, Marine\nResources and Ocean Survey, Meteorology;\nand Environment\nImportant to all these\nfields of investigation is the development of\nnew techniques for the interpretation of data\nto be \tused in conjunction with those already\nestablished\n\nMultistage (multilevel) sampling techniques\nhave been used successfully in timber class id\xc2\xad\nentification and volume estimation, and for\xc2\xad\nest fire fuel mapping and modeling (Nichols).\nThis technique involves the integration of\nsatellite, aircraft, and ground data and has\nproven to be an efficient means of gathering\nforest data. As in the case of crop inventory\n\nThe following synopses illustrate some of the\nmajor applications devised to date, the techniques employed in developing them, and in\nparenthesis, the names of NASA Principal\nInvestigators reporting them. A complete\nlist of Pis is included in Appendix J.\n\n3-1\n\n\nmap of the region using the grid coordinate\nannotation of the imagery, with each class\nA land use map, "Northern\ncolor-coded\nMegalopolis" (United States), contained the\nfollowing classes: Commercial and Indus\xc2\xad\ntrial, Residential High Density, Residential\nLow Density, Transportation, Developed\nOpen Space (urban), Rural Open Land (with\nand without residential), Agricultural, Wood\xc2\xad\nlands, Marshlands, Sand and Rock Outcrops,\nand Water\n\napplications, stratification has also been useful as a basic tool in forest survey applications.\nSoil association identification and mapping\nhave been accomplished by both photointerpretation and ADP techniques. The procedures used generally include stratification\nusing vegetation, topography and temporal\ndata as information sources (Drew) In addition, density slicing has proven to be especially useful in the mapping of soil associations\nfound in predominantly sandy regions. Color\ncomposite images have been used to identify\ndifferent features of soil associations in a wide\nvariety of climatic, geographic and topographic conditions (Westin). Pure ADP techniques using training sets and pixel count\nhave been used to discriminate organic and\nmineral soils (Landgrebe)\n\nOther experimenters (Erb, Thompson, Raje)\nhave used computer analysis of the four MSS\nspectral bands to automatically identify the\nclass of land use through definition of decis\xc2\xad\nion boundaries in this four-dimensional space,\nand correlation of each four-dimensional sig\xc2\xad\nnature cluster to a known (ground truth)\nland use test site. This technique can be ap\xc2\xad\nplied in both unsupervised and supervised\nmodes of operation.\n\nRange cover and forage production applications have involved the use of regression an\xc2\xad\nalyses and band-ratioing techniques. CCT-extracted band 5 radiance values have been\nfound to be strongly correlated with vegetative biomass (Drew), and the ratioing of\n\nUnsupervised clustering is accomplished auto\xc2\xad\nmatically by defining the boundary vectors of\nsignificant clusters of data samples in the pop\xc2\xad\nulation. Usually this is done in response to\na query for a specific number (n) of clusters\nthat the user is interested in defining, e.g,\n"n" land use classes. After processing and\ncoding, the user must correlate the spatial\narrangement in the hardcopy output of the\nmachine to known ground truth test sites in\norder to interpret the data. Additional it\xc2\xad\n\nbands 5 and 7 has produced measurement ac\xc2\xad\n\ncuracies to within 1 0% of the true value 9 5 %\nof the time (Rouse). A related application,\nmapping according to the\nvegetation\nUNESCO scheme published in 1974, has been\nshown to be feasible using Landsat data\n(Williams).\n\nerations are often necessary to correct for er\xc2\xad\n\nap\xc2\xad\nStress detection is an important potential\nplication of Landsat data that has, at best,\nbeen limitedly successful.\n\nrors of commission or omission by trimming\nthe decision boundaries of each cluster.\n\nSupervised interactive classification is gener\xc2\xad\nally performed on smaller capacity machines\nusing extensive software and a real-time CRT\ndisplay. It is usually done for each class\nthrough selection by the operator of a spatial\ncluster of data samples that he knows to best\nrepresent each land use class of interest The\nmachine reads these data points, statistically\nanalyzes them and stores the boundary char\xc2\xad\nacteristics of the four-dimensional radiometric\ncluster for classification of all other data\n\n3.2.2 Land Use Survey and Mapping\nVarious Landsat experimenters have demonstrated that broad but meaningful regional\nland use classification maps can be generated.\nThe technique used by one experimenter\n(Simpson) consisted of tracing polygons\n(shapes containing a predominant land use\ncharacteristic) from 1 1,000,000 scale imagery. These land use polygon chips were\nthen transferred to a standard geographical\n\n3-2\n\n\npoints within the particular data frame The\nprocess is repeated for each of the classes; the\ntheme clusters are trimmed until the complete\ndata frame is classified.\n\nhancement, and digital image processing have\nbeen especially successful in several applica\xc2\xad\ntions. Photolnear maps compiled from an\xc2\xad\nalysis of MSS bands 5 and 7 have been the\nbasis for identifying potential resource areas\nAnalysis of areas characterized by a high\nnumber of linears or linear intersections plus\ncomparison with known resource locations\nand the use of additional supportive data, has\nbeen the method used most often for finding\nnew resource potentials. Low-sun-angle and\nsnow-enhanced imagery are frequently found\noptimal for linears mapping A multilevel ap\xc2\xad\nproach, combining Landsat-derived maps\nwith aircraft photography, geophysical data,\nand ground truth, continues to yield the best\nresults.\n\nIt should be noted that the success of these\ntechniques depends to a large extent on establhshing and surveying a sufficient number\nof control test sites, with ground truth for\neach class, to permit confident visual signature extension.\nThe same manual and digital processing techniques described can be used to outline the\nnatural resources in large regions, the difference being in the selection of polygon characteristics.\nLandsat images also have been used by professional cartographers (Colvocoresses, Wray,\nMcEwen) to generate experimental photomaps at scales as large as 1:250,000, using\nthe fundamental false-color composite product as the base material and fine-lining over\nit all other nonphysical information normally\nfound on maps, such as political boundaries,\nnames and identifications, and reference coordinates. The use of such imagery for periodic updates of maps at scales as large as\n1-100,000 has been found to be of value by\ncartographers.\n\nA new technique to complement standard in\xc2\xad\nterpretive techniques, using Landsat spectral\nband ratios to distinguish potential mim\xc2\xad\neralized zones, has also been developed\n(Rowan).\nWith this technique, enhanced\ncolor and black and white images are pro\xc2\xad\nduced by digital computer ratioing and con\xc2\xad\ntrast stretching (e.g , band 4/5 blue, band\n5/6 yellow, band 6/7 magenta) Investigators\nhave succeeded in mapping hydrothermally al\xc2\xad\ntered areas and in locating potential copper\nporphyry deposits using this method (Sch\xc2\xad\nmidt). In addition, a technique for digital\nspatial filtering of imagery data has been used\nto enhance linear patterns, as well as a tech\xc2\xad\nnique for the electronic enhancement of\ninears (Goetz). Still another procedure em\xc2\xad\nploys standard false-color composites, which\nare especially valuable for lithologic mapping\nand in recognizing anomalous surface tonal\ncharacteristi s.\n\n3.2.3 \tMineral Resources, Geological, \n\nStructural and Landform Survey \n\n\nIn the area of mineral and petroleum resources, Landsat data have been effectively\nused to pinpoint areas of prospective mineral\n(non-metallic and metallic) and hydrocarbon\nresources of economic value. Standard photointerprtation of imagery to identify significant structural features (linears, faults, etc.)\nhas been the most popular approach, although\nadvanced interpretive techniques, including\nphotographic enhancement, electronic en-\n\nRecent studies have indicated that Landsat\nimagery also has many applications in en\xc2\xad\ngineering and environmental geology. Po\xc2\xad\ntential ground water resources can be id\xc2\xad\nentified by a combination of structural an\xc2\xad\nalysis of the imagery, vegetation mapping and\nsurficial moisture content mapping The lo\xc2\xad\ncation of potential ground water sources has\n\n3-3\n\n\nbeen\nachieved\nusing\ncomputer-ratioed,\n"stretched" images, coupled with optical-dir-\n\nsessing areal snow extent (Wisnet, Barnes),\nparticularly .in small river basins (<3500\nkm 2 ). The MSS has also been employed to\nmonitor glaciers, predict snowmelt runoff,\nand detect so-called "melting" snow surfaces.\nSatellite data of snow conditions are useful\nto many federal and state agencies, especi\xc2\xad\nally where snowmelt significantly contri\xc2\xad\nbutes to spring and summer river discharges\n(Schumann). Such information permits more\nefficient utilization of snow as a valuable\nresource; e g., flood prediction, hydroelectric\npower generation, and water supply Landsat\nimagery has proven to be a useful tool for\nmapping snow areal extent and relating this\nparameter to seasonal runoff.\n\nectional "filtering" (Goetz). Standard falsecolor composites and digitally processed color\nratioed-stretched images have proven useful in\nmapping differing soil moisture conditions\nalong the route of a planned highway\n(Krinsley). Identification of fractures andlinears using bands 5 and 7 can be used to map\npotential landslide areas, unsuspected active\nfaults adjacent to populated areas or highway\nroutes and fractures cutting underground miing areas. Photomosaics compiled using Landsat imagery may reveal many previously unknown active faults (Gedney), and extensive\nerosion areas can be mapped using standard\nimagery (Morrison).\n\nUsing black and white 9 5-inch transparencies\n(band 7) and conventional photointerpreta\xc2\xad\ntion techniques, Norwegian investigators\n(Odegaard, Ostrem) were able to use snow\ncover area determination, in conjunction with\nan established network of ground-based snow\npillow sensors, to accurately forecast sea\xc2\xad\nsonal snowmelt runoff, thereby maximizing\nthe utility of a hydroelectric facility and at\nthe same time minimizing possible flood dam\xc2\xad\nage below the dam.\n\nSatellite imagery lends itself well to the field\nof geological mapping and interpretation. In\nareas where rock exposures are good because\nof low vegetation cover, major geologic features such as folds, fault offsets, volcanic\nflows, contacts between different units, and\nmany types of landform can be effectively\nmapped. The resolution and synoptic view\nof the imagery allows these structures to be\nmapped using standard Landsat black and\nwhite prints and transparencies to scales of\n1:250,000.\nComputer-produced geologic\nmaps can be generated at even larger scales,\nprovided sufficient data are available. Landsat-derived geologic maps can be compiled in2\nto detailed structures, landforms, or lithologic\n(rock unit, but not type) maps. Results of\nstudies reveal that present Landsat resolution\nprecludes the identification of particular rock\ntypes, by lithology or by chemical composition Only a few rock types are readily and\nconsistently recognizable (e g., basalts in\nflows) (Blodget).\n\nInvestigations have shown that the high ab\xc2\xad\nsorption characteristic of band 7 (Cowell,\nHiger, Anderson) makes possible the accur\xc2\xad\nate delineation of surface water boundaries\nThis band has been especially useful in map\xc2\xad\nping flood inundated areas as well as in de\xc2\xad\nlineating areas of potential inundation that\nare characterized by high soil moisture con\xc2\xad\ntent. Landsat data have been used on an op\xc2\xad\nerational basis to map the extent of flood\nwaters in parts of the United States and many\nforeign countries. Specifically, Landsat data\nhave been useful for rapid evaluation of dam\xc2\xad\nage due to flood water, assessment of flood\nwater control projects, land use planning, and\nfor reservoir management.\n\n3.2.4 Water Resources\nThe management of the earth\'s fresh water resources can also be aided through the use of\nLandsat data. Considering, for example, snow\nsurvey versus snowmelt runoff prediction,\nLandsat is providing data for accurately as-\n\nThe digital processing of Landsat data also al\xc2\xad\nlows the rapid analysis of hydrologic land use\npatterns for watershed surveys (Blanchard,\n\n3-4\n\n\nWagner). Extractable land use classes include\naccurate measurement of surface water, agriculture, urban, residential, forest, marsh,\nand flood-prone areas. Thematic classifications of watersheds have proven useful for\nmonitoring watershed development as well as\nplanning zoning legislation\n\nwater depth information from MSS CCTs\nhave been developed. (A single channel me\xc2\xad\nthod, a ratio method, and an optimum decis\xc2\xad\nion boundary method)\nThe ratio method\n(using bands 4 and 5 where possible) gives the\nbest depth values and tends to negate adverse\neffects of differing bottom conditions (sand,\nmud, etc.). With adequate ground truth, an\nabsolute depth chart can be constructed.\n\nFor the first time, Landsat MSS satellite data\nhave provided environmentalists with the cap\xc2\xad\nability to monitor the water quality of large\necosystems (Yarger, Rogers), such as rivers\nand bays, on a time-effective basis Although\nquality analysis of aqueous solutions involves\na variety of analytical procedures, a limited\nnumber of quality parameters can be monitored via satellite remote sensing. At present, the detectable quality parameters are related to suspended sediment in water. Digital\nprocessing of Landsat data has been used to\ndifferentiate between organic and inorganic\nsediment in inland waters. Although it is not\npresently possible to establish detailed quantitative relationships between sediment loads\nand MSS reflectances, it is possible to detect\nnuisance-level water pollution levels,\n\nMonitoring the location and movement of sea\nice has special implications for arctic sea navi\xc2\xad\ngation (Barnes). Here, the method of multi\xc2\xad\nspectral analysis is useful for distinguishing ice\nfloes from surrounding brash ice and ice\ncakes, for detecting puddling on the ice sur\xc2\xad\nface as opposed to cracks or fractures through\nthe ice and for identifying broken cloud fields\nover ice surfaces. In areas of nearly solid ice\ncover, greater detail is evident in band 7 pri\xc2\xad\nmarily because differences in reflectance be\xc2\xad\ntween ice floes, brash ice and cracks and\nopenings are greater Also, reflectance varia\xc2\xad\ntions within some ice floes, which are evident\nin band 7, may be associated with hummocks,\nridges or refrozen cracks. Bands 4 and 5 ap\xc2\xad\npear to be better for mapping ice boundaries,\nwhereas band 7 provides valuable informa\xc2\xad\ntion on ice type and ice surface features. Ice\nfeatures as small as 20 to 100 meters across\nhave been detected Synoptic coverage and\nlarge image side lap at high latitudes permits\nrepetitive coverage for monitoring changes\nand movements for periods of up to four\ndays.\n\nLandsat investigators have reported success in\ndetecting algal blooms and areas of high inorganic sediment in surface waters. The detection of algal blooms can be related to water of\nhigh organic nutrient content (nitrogen and\nphosphorous) resulting from insufficient\nwastewater treatment and agricultural runoff\nDetection of high inorganic sediment has been\nuseful in locating areas of inadequate er\xc2\xad\nosion control and monitoring sludge disposal\noperations.\n\nBiologically rich areas of the ocean have been\nlocated by enhancing the low spectral radi\xc2\xad\nances that are characteristic of water bodies\non CCTs (Szekielda). This brings out consid\xc2\xad\nerable detail in surface water that is un\xc2\xad\navailable on the original images. Upwelling\nwater (Hendrickson) appears as an area of\nlower reflectance. Such areas may disrupt\nflow patterns of turbidity streamers in water\nmoving under tidal influence along a coast\nAlgal blooms in band 6 reveal more detail\nthan in bands 4 or 5 (Strong) By comparing\nspectral signatures from turbid and chloro\xc2\xad\n\n3.2.5 Marine Resources and Ocean Survey\nLandsat data have been used successfully in\nseveral aspects of marine resources studies.\nIn the area of bathymetry, MSS data have,\nbeen used to measure depths up to 20 meters\nwith t10% accuracy and up to 40 meters\nwith \xc2\xb1 2 0 %\naccuracy\nin\nclear\nwater\n(Polcyn). Three methods for extraction of\n\n3-5\n\n\nphyll-rich ocean water, a process can be derived to separate the two effects In one\nLandsat experiment (Maughan, Stevenson),\ndistribution of photographically detected adult menhaden was significantly correlated\nwith seccht disk visibility, surface salinity,\nwater color and depth. All detected menhaden schools were located in areas of lowest\nband 5 image density.\n\nIn air pollution studies, the resolution and\nspatial extent of the imagery allow detailed\nobservation of a "regional air shed" or air\nquality control region. Air sheds have been\nidentified and observed (Lyons) by noting the\ndirection and extent of pollution plumes gen\xc2\xad\nerated by an industrial complex. Experience\nhas shown that these particulate plumes can\nbe seen best over water in either band 5 or\n6, due to optimum contrast between the low\nalbedo water surface and higher albedo parti\xc2\xad\nculate plume. Detection of smoke plumes\nover land is more difficult unless the plumes\nare unusually dense. Land surfaces are gen\xc2\xad\nerally much brighter than water and exhibit\nmarked spectral and seasonal variations in rad\xc2\xad\niance. Observing the intra-or inter-regional\ntransport of pollutants can be useful in assess\xc2\xad\ning a region\'s ability to meet and maintain air\nquality standards. The feasibility of this type\nof air pollution monitoring, under certain\nconditions, has been demonstrated.\n\nSurveying currents and the results of ocean\ndynamics worldwide would be an important\ncontribution to navigation as well as to the\nharvesting of the sea\'s resources. Some findings to date (Pine, Sharma) indicate that turbid waters introduced into relatively clear\noceanic water at the mouths of estuaries serve\nas a natural tracer to delineate circulation patterns, and that complex, fast-changing microcirculation patterns develop during each tidal\ncycle (Strong, Hendrickson). Conventional\nship data reveal overall circulation but fail to\nshow sub-gyres as seen in Landsat imagery.\nBand 5 gives the most accurate representation\nfor suspended sediment boundaries, bands\n4 and 5 give indications of sediment concentrations. Bands 6 and 7 delineate the shoreline, and discriminate water from land in\nmarsh areas.\nImages have been analyzed\n(Maul) to delineate surface and subsurface\nsediment distribution and to differentiate\nnearshore morphologic units and to map current effects (Klemas). It is also possible to\nmeasure the seaward extent of suspended sediment transport and to differentiate sediment\nlevels within individual lobes.\n\nIn cloud studies, the structure and arrange\xc2\xad\nment of clouds from small cumulus cells to\nthin cirrus can be uniquely observed with\nLandsat. Its resolution provides details that\nare not obtainable with conventional mete\xc2\xad\norological satellites. The multispectral imag\xc2\xad\ning capability of the imagery is most useful to\nthe meteorologist for selectively penetra\xc2\xad\nting haze and thin clouds, revealing cloud\nshadows, delineating snow cover from vegeta\xc2\xad\ntion, and demarcating land and water boun\xc2\xad\ndaries. Using the multispectral images, vary\xc2\xad\nmg structures of the clouds can be observed.\nBand 7 effectively penetrates most haze and\nthin clouds, revealing only the thicker cloud\nstructures, while band 4 displays the entire\ncloud or haze pattern. Complex mesoscale\ngravity waves in low stratus and fog have been\nobserved (Lyons) over lakes, and cloud de\xc2\xad\ntail apparent in inadvertent weather modifica\xc2\xad\ntion, lake front breezes, mesoscale spiral vor\xc2\xad\ntices and lake shore convergence and snows\nhave been revealed. The possibility of detect\xc2\xad\ning thin cirrus related to areas of clear air\nturbulence and the core of a jet stream has\nbeen shown (Tsuchiya); investigators have al\xc2\xad\nso observed localized patterns of orographic\n\n3.2.6 Meteorology\nThe primary meteorological applications of\nLandsat data have been in the study of mesoscale phenomena. The resolution, spectral\nbands and synoptic view of Landsat provide a\nperspective of localized meteorological patterns that cannot be obtained with conventional weather satellites. Most investigators in\nmeteorology have analyzed Landsat imagery\nthrough photointerpretation techniques and\nmany have also successfully collected meteorological data via the Landsat DCS.\n3-6\n\n\nrepetitive basis, and therefore allow evalua\xc2\xad\ntion and even quantification of environmental\ntrends and changes caused by man\n\nlifting, thermal convergence and energy balance relationships.\nInvestigators (Bowden, Hollyday) have viewed\nthe actions and aftereffects of localized severe\nweather The Landsat view of a Santa Ana\nwind in progress, for example, gave additional\ninsights into the general flow patterns of this\nintense, damaging wind. Particular areas of\nheavy wind erosion could be found by tracing\nthe origins of large dust plumes In another\ninstance, the path of a particularly devestating\ntornado was readily observable in the Landsat data of a national forest.\n\n3.2.7 Environment\n\nVegetation classification and mapping from\nLandsat imagery are useful for many types of\nenvironmental studies. Using manual tech\xc2\xad\nniques, investigators (J. Anderson, McGinnies)\nhave mapped the vegetation associations vital\nto wildlife habitat. Digital classification of\nmultispectral data from CCTs has been of\ngreat value in monitoring large areas for\nchanges in wildlife habitat conditions such as\nvegetation density and water quantity (Lent).\nThe satellite data reveal vegetation type and\nvitality of coastal and inland wetlands, which\nare of great importance to migratory water\xc2\xad\nfowl and to many aquatic and marine crea\xc2\xad\ntures. In addition, manual comparison of im\xc2\xad\nages has been valuable for monitoring the en\xc2\xad\ncroachment of man upon the coastal zone\n(Yunghans). Changes due to development, as\nsmall as two acres, have been detected. Also\nof significance to wildlife studies is the cap\xc2\xad\nability of mapping permafrost zones in arctic\nregions (D. Anderson), which are almost in\xc2\xad\naccessible except through satellite sensing.\nVegetation maps of warmer climes have been\nused to detect breeding sites for locusts and\nother agricultural pests (Pedgley)\n\nFrom the p]btforn of the earth-orbiting satellite, valuable information can be collected\non the quality of this planet\'s environmental\nsystems. While the satellite\'s multispectral\nsensor bandwidths were selected principally\nto reveal the content, quantity and condition\nof vegetated surfaces, they also yield information on water quantity and quality and on\nland surfaces barren of vegetation. The synoptic scale of Landsat imagery is valuable for\nintegration of various simultaneous conditions\nand changes within an entire region, allowing\nholistic monitoring of environmental systems.\nAt the same time, the acre-sized resolution\ncell provides a level of detail that is valuable\nfor quantitative environmental monitoring,\nPerhaps the most significant characteristic of\nthe satellite data isthat they are available on a\n\nBecause digital analysis retains the spectral\nand spatial resolution of each pixel, this\nmeans of Landsat data analysis has been val\xc2\xad\nuable for water quality studies. Reflectance\nlevel, particularly of band 5, can be used to\nmonitor turbidity content throughout a body\nof water where a few surface readings have\nbeen taken. Digital classification of repetitive\nscenes can show sedimentation trends in\nlakes, and dispersal patterns of outfalls\n(Rogers, Wezernak, Yost, Fontanel). Digital\nland use classification of watersheds has been\nshown to be a reliable indirect indicator of\nlake and reservoir quality, and a means of id\xc2\xad\nentifying areas where water quality changes\nare likely to occur. Thus satellite imagery can\nbe used to detect industrial and municipal\nwaste dumps in nearshore waters, and some\n\nOne of the most effective means of collecting\nground truth meteorological data for many\nLandsat investigations has been with the instrumented data collection platform (DCP).\nSeven Landsat DCPs were utilized (Kahan)\nin a major winter weather modification program in the Colorado River Basin. The DCPs\nreturned hydrometerological data such as\nwind speed and direction, temperature, humidity, snow depth and density, and streamflow. Through a communications network,\ncollected data were available to the users\nwithin 3 to 8 hours of DCP transmission.\n\n3-7\n\n\ncorrelations to chlorophyll content have been\nreported (Rogers, Yunghans, Klemas),\n\n3.3 SPECTRAL BAND INFORMATION DE\xc2\xad\nSCRIPTORS\n\nMultispectral Landsat imagery shows a high\ncontrast between vegetated and non-vegetated\nsurfaces, and thus is readily applicable to\nmonitoring large scale construction projects\nand surface mining operations. Black and\nwhite band 7 images have been used on a regular basis to update maps of strip-mining\nregions (Sweet). Digital classification of both\nmultiple band and band-ratioed imagery\nallows distinction of stripped and partially\nrevegetated surfaces, and can be used to measure the area affected by mining operations\nwith a high degree of accuracy (Rogers, A.\nAnderson).\nLandsat imagery has been used to detect and\nmonitor certain types of large scale environmental changes, such as beach erosion\n(Maruyasu), forest fires (A Anderson), and\nfloods. Investigations are continuing to determine the feasibility of using Landsat to study\n\nThe Landsat MSS remotely senses energy re\xc2\xad\nflected from the earth in four different ranges\nof the electromagnetic spectrum. The radia\xc2\xad\ntion sensed by the scanner varies with the\ncharacteristics of the emitting (reflecting)\nbody as to wavelength and intensity, but all\nspectral returns are converted into digital\nvalues in their respective bands The digital\nvalue depends upon the strength of the signal\nreceived. When these digital values are repro\xc2\xad\nduced as imagery each band presents its own\nunique picture of the same scene. Further,\ncertain objects in a scene may yield more information about themselves in one spectral\nband than in another With this background,\nTable 3-1 was prepared to aid users in select\xc2\xad\ning the band that will probably be most help\xc2\xad\nful to them in their particular area of study.\nThe chart represents a consensus of the find\xc2\xad\nings and opinions of the various investigators\nworking in the areas encompassed by the dis\xc2\xad\n\nair pollution, oil spills, vegetation disease and\nvarious types of water pollution.\n\nciplines discussed here. The U.S\nArmy Corps of Engineers has a continuing\nneed for basic environmental information for\nplanning projects and assessing their resulting\nimpact They have requested NASA to as\xc2\xad\nsess an atlas, "Inventory of Basic Enviroh\xc2\xad\nmental Data, South Louisiana," produced\nfrom formerly existing data, to determine\nhow much of the data could be updated using\nremote sensing. Data regarding land use and\nvegetation classification were identfied as\ncandidates for automated classification ut\xc2\xad\nilizing remote sensing data.\n\n3.4 \tAPPLICATIONS SYSTEMSVERIFICATION AND TRANSFER PROJECTS\n(ASVTs)\nCertain quasi-operational projects have been\nselected by NASA to demonstrate the applicability of Landsat data to the solutions of\nresource management problems These projects are called Applications Systems Verification and Transfer projects (ASVTs).\nWhen each test project is completed a decision will be made as to whether or not it is\ncost effective to go operational with the\nLandsat information. A description of four\nof these ASVTs is given here. It is anticipated that more ASVTs will be identified in\nthe future\n\nA number of Earth Resources Laboratory\nSustaining Research and Technology projects\nin South Louisiana and Mississippi, and as\xc2\xad\nsociated software and hardware projects, have\nprovided the basis for developing an auto\xc2\xad\nmated system to meet the user agency re\xc2\xad\nquirements South Louisiana is asuitable test\narea because of logistics, applicability of past\nstudies, user agency interest, and predom\xc2\xad\ninance of marsh in the area.\n\n3.4 1 \tSnow Cover and Runoff Prediction\n\nThe object of this project is to measure various snow parameters with remote sensors and\nuse the measurements as inputs to runoff\ncomputations.\nData from various remote\n-ensing platforms and various sensors, cover\xc2\xad\ning different portions of the electromagnetic\nspectrum, will be subjected to analysis using\nstandard photointerpretation techniques and\nmore sophisticated computer techniques.\nData will be collected in four different snow\nareas in the U.S.\nthe Pacific Northwest,\nCalifornia, Arizona, and Colorado. The effort\nwill be implemented in cooperation with the\nfollowing federal, state and local water resources management agencies:\nThe U S.\nArmy Corps of Engineers, the U.S. Geological\nSurvey, the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, the U.S. Forest Ser\xc2\xad\nvice, the U.S. Soil Conservation Service, the\nBonneville Power Administration, the Arizona Salt River Project, the California Depart\xc2\xad\nment of Water Resources and the Colorado\nDivision of Water Resources\n\nSimplified pattern recognition software, suit\xc2\xad\nable for general purpose computers, and a low\ncost image display system will be utilized\nThe system will accept satellite and aircraft\nmultispectral scanner data and other types of\ndata for correlative purposes. The system de\xc2\xad\nfinition documentation and training will be\noriented to user implementation of the\nsystem. The demonstration tests will prove\nthe feasibility of using the system to update\na variety of environmental thematic data;\ne.g., marsh salinity regions.\n3.4.3 \tNatural Resources Information\nSystem\nThe object of this project is to test and dem\xc2\xad\nonstrate an automated natural resources in\xc2\xad\n\n3-9\n\n\nventory system based on remotely sensed data\noriented to state or regional use, and directed\nat specific applications. States and other\ngoverning bodies have a need for accurate, up\xc2\xad\nto-date natural resources inventory information for managing resources, optimizing\ngrowth and development and minimizing en\xc2\xad\nvironmental impact The large geographical\nareas involved make surface inventory difficult and encourage the use of remote sensing.\nThe resulting large quantities of data imply\nthe desirability of automatic processing, access and correlative capability. The effort for\nthe past three years has been oriented to the\ndevelopment of software, hardware and disciplinary techniques suitable for incorporation into such an automated system, with the\nState of Mississippi as the test area. This\nsystem will also use simplified pattern recognition software and a low cost image display\nsystem. The project will demonstrate the\nsystem in state facilities and will be oriented\nto the production of inventories of timber,\nland use, water resources and wildlife habitat\nIt will require the use of a prototype operational semi-automatic system, with Landsat\nMSS and ground truth data.\n\nfollowing the use of such information through\nthe decision-making process associated with\neach application.\n3.4.4 \t Large Area Crop Inventory Experiment\n(LAClE)\nLACIE is an interagency experimental dem\xc2\xad\nonstration project involving the U.S. Depart\xc2\xad\nment of Agriculture (USDA), the National\nOceanic and Atmospheric Agency (NOAA) of\nthe U.S. Department of Commerce, and\nNASA The project isdesigned to test the de\xc2\xad\ngree to which computer-assisted analysis of\nspace-acquired data may enhance the USDA\'s\noperational crop forecasting programs. It is\nintended to demonstrate the capability of re\xc2\xad\nlatively new remote sensing and data process\xc2\xad\ning systems in combination with existing tech\xc2\xad\nniques and historical data to forecast the pro\xc2\xad\nduction of an important world crop Wheat\nhas been selected as the test crop for the\nLACIE demonstration.\nLACIE will utilize Landsat in conjunction\nwith meteorological satellites and conven\xc2\xad\ntional meteorological and climatological data\nsources Landsat multispectral data gathered\nrepetitively over selected sites will be class\xc2\xad\nified and used to measure crop area, while\nmeteorological data are used in statistical\nmodels to predict crop yield. Area and yield\nestimates will then be combined to arrive at\ncrop production estimates.\n\nThe utilityof inventories made with remotely\nsensed data will be greatly enhanced in specific applications through the development of\nan information system designed to combine\nnatural resources inventory data with other\ndata; e.g., soils, climate, and population den\xc2\xad\nsities. Such an information system will be developed within a framework that allows geographic referencing. A utility assessment, including cost-benefit studies, will be made by\n\nThe initial phase of LACIE will concentrate\nprimarily on the United States and Canada.\nIt will then be extended to include the major\nwheat growing regions of the world\n\n3-10\n\n\nSECTION4\nOUTPUT DATA PRODUCTS\n\nduring the process of videotape-to-film\nconversion The term "system-corrected\nimagery," which refers to these correc\xc2\xad\ntions, applies to all imagery.\n\n4.1 INTRODUCTION\nLandsat data products produced by the\nNASA Image Processing Facility (IPF) at\nGSFC are discussed in this section. The image is\npositioned within the writing area so that\nwhen the registration marks from two or\n\n9.5 INCH\nPOSITIVE\nTRIPLETS\nCOLOR COMPOSITE PRINTER\nSEQUENTIALLY EXPOSE\nBLACK AND WHITE TRIPLETS\n\nmore spectral images are superimposed, the\nimagery will be registered. The format center of a\nscene imaged at the same time by both the\nRBV and MSS will be identical. The gray\nscale gives the relationship between a level of\ngray on the image and the electron beam\ndensity used to expose the original image. The\nelectron beam density is related to the sensor\nsignal voltage which, in turn, Is related to the\nenergy incident on the sensor,\n\nThe gray scale tablet is a macroscale tablet\nand cannot be used reliably for microscale\nimage radiometry, because the areas, on\nthe order of a few picture elements, are\nsubject to influence by neighboring areas\n(modulation\ntransfer\nfunction\neffects,\nchemical development adjacency effects)\nand do not supply enough data points to\naverage noise down to a low figure.\n\nThe annotation gray scale for MSS imagery\ncorresponds to zero radiance at step 15 (black\non positives) and maximum radiance as given\nby Figure C-5 at step 1 (white on positives),\nThe radiance varies linearly with gray step\ntransmission between these values with the\ndifference between each step corresponding\nto 1/14th of the maximum radiance.\n\nThe dimensions of the gray scale and alpha\xc2\xad\nnumeric annotation blocks are shown in\nFigure 4-8.\n\n4.2.2.4 Alphanumeric Annotation\nFigure 4-9 details the type of alphanumeric\nannotation shown at the bottom of Figure\n4-5 Items a through i explain the data con\xc2\xad\ntamed in this annotation.\n\nThe transmission of the steps in the RBV\nannotation gray scale varies linearly with the\ncamera voltage, between 320 and 1100 millivolts The voltage difference between each\nstep is 1/14th of [1100 - 320] or 55.7 milli\xc2\xad\nvolts. These tapes\nare standard 0.5-inch polyester-base magnetic\ntapes, whose physical characteristics are\ngiven in Figure 4-11 and Table 4-2, logical\ncharacteristics are discussed in Subsection\n4 3.2. One, two, or four CCTs, comprising\na set, contain one scene of digital imagery.\nThe external label on each tape contains\nthe arrangement and type of information\nshown in Figure 4-12. Note that the MSS images are grouped\nby spectral band, that is, sequentially adjacent\nimages on the roll are for sequential geographical areas. These images are followed by\nthe same sequence of adjacent images in the\nnext spectral band, etc.\n4.2.3.2 \t Landsat-C\nLandsat-C imagery data will be processed\nthrough the Image Processing Facility (IPF),\nconverted to High Density Tape and supplied\nto the Landsat Data Distribution Centers in\ndigital form either via communication links\nor shipment of High Density Tape copies\nHigh Density Tape, computer compatible\ntape and photographic products will also be\nprovided by the IPF to selected special tasks\nand special users\n\n4.3.1 CCT Physical Format\nCCTs are in two basic physical formats\n1. each is one recording position\nin length, extending across the tape,\nperpendicular to the direction of tape\n\nmovement.) \t\n\nThe full frame 185.2-by-185.2 km (100-by\xc2\xad\n100 nm) image is segmented into four 46.3\xc2\xad\nby-185.2 km (25-by-100 nm) strips in the\ndirection of spacecraft heading, for con\xc2\xad\nversion into CCT format.\n\n2. \tSeven-Track, 800 bpi - The seven-track\nCCT contains packed binary video\ndata and packed EBCDIC alphanumeric\ndata.\nIn the "packed" configuration,\ntlree 8-bit bytes are contained in four\nframes\nThe record layout and bit\nstructure are identical to the layout\nand structure of the nine-track CCT.\n\n9-TRACK COPY CCT\nW0 # ..\n....\nSCENEI\n.-.\nREELS 1&2 OF 4\n\nDATE..\nUSER11\n\n/.\n\n4.3.2.1\n\nThe video data are spectrally interleaved The\ninterleaving is done in groups of 8 bytes,\n2 bytes from each spectral band. The absence\nof an expected scan line is identified on a\nCCT by the use of a special code that does\nnot occur in ordinary imagery Missing scan\nlines are identified by the occurrence of the\nbyte X \'CC\' (11001100) at the start of the\nordinary scan line byte sequence on the CCT\nAn ordinary scan lne byte sequence is de\xc2\xad\nfined as the first quarter scan line of video\ndata.\n\n.\n\nI\n\n.1..\n\n......--\n\nMB\n\nBPI\n\n.\n\nVideo Data Format and Content\n\n.\n\nRadiometric calibration data for each spectral\n\nband is also inserted as a 56-byte calibration\n\nFigure 4-12 MSS CCT External Tape Label\n\n4-10\n\n\ngroup following each block of 3n 8-byte\ngroups of interleaved video data The letter\nIn"is used to represent that multiplier of\nthe number "24" which is needed to calculate\nthe adjusted video data scan line length\n(LLA). The resulting multiple of 24 is constrained to be the smallest such multiple\nwhich is at least as great as the maximum\nline length code (LLC) determined for a\ngiven scene, plus 6. All line lengths are, of\ncourse, measured in pixels, or bytes. Additional detail will be found in Appendix D\nof this handbook.\n\n1, whether the data are from Landsat 1 or\nLandsat 2\nCharacters 13-16 contain the\nsequencing numbers, e g., 1 of 2, 2 of 2,\nwhich would distinguish the tapes in a set\nof two. Characters 17-18 contain the data\nrecord length in binary, i.e., the length of\nthe adjusted scan line plus 56 bytes of calibra\xc2\xad\ntion information Characters 19-26 contain\nthe binary frame ID, which is the binary\nrepresentation of the scene/frame ID and\nmust be broken into days, hours, minutes,\nseconds, etc, to be read. Characters 27-28,\nthe binary strip ID, are not used and are\nset to zero. Characters 29-36 contain the\nimage annotation tape (IAT) ID, which\nidentifies the IAT used in making the CCT\nCharacters 37-38 contain the MSS data mode/\ncorrection code, which is a digital word that\nindicates the characteristics of the data\nsuch as decompression, calibration, and line\nlength adjustment. (See Table 4-4 for the\ncomplete definition of the MSS data mode/\ncorrection code.) Characters 39-40 contain\nthe MSS adjusted line length. All of the\nabove information is defined in more detail\nin Table 4-4.\n\nFigures 4-13 through 4-15 illustrate the CCT\nvideo data format and content, symbols used\nin these illustrations are defined in Table 4-3.\nThe video data word consists of eight bits, of\nwhich only six are used if the data mode is\nlinear and seven are used if the data mode is\ndecompressed All video data-bit words are\nright-justified. Bits which are not used for\nvideo data are used as flags, as when\n11111111 is used as the spatial registration\nfill character (X \'FF\'). The decompressed\nmode arises when data has been transmitted\nfrom the satellite in acompressed mode. The\ndecompressed mode yields radiance values\nrepresented by byte values ranging from 0\nto 127, instead of 0 to 63, as with the linear\nmode\n\n4.3.2 3 Annotation Record\nThe annotation record contains binary and\nEBCDIC data that provide information about\nthe scene such as the format center, nadir and\nsun elevation This record also includes tick\nmark location information that associates\nthe digitized scene with the latitude and\nlongitude coordinate system. The annotation\nrecord is the second record of each file, thus\noccurring once or more per tape dependant\nupon format, and contains 624 characters.\nThis record is actually a composite of two\nrecords taken directly from the image annota\xc2\xad\ntion tape. The first 144 characters comprise\nthe annotation block, and the next 480\ncharacters comprise the image location\nrecord Figure 4-17 defines the sequence of\ninformation in the annotation record.\n\n4.3.2.2 Identification (ID) Record\nThe ID record contains a combination of\nbinary and EBCDIC information that is\nused to identify the video data of each file.\nThis 40-byte record is therefore the first\nrecord on a CCT, and appears thereafter\nat the start of each file if there is more than\none file on the tape. Figure 4-16 shows\nthe organization of the ID record.\nThe first word in the ID record is the scene/\nframe ID, given in terms of days, hours,\nminutes, and tens of seconds since launch In\naddition, this record indicates the spectral\nband (Landsats 1 and 2 = bands 4-7, set to\nzero), sequential subframe ID (subframes\nunavailable, set to zero), and by character\n\n4.3.2.3.1 Annotation Data Block\nThe information included in the image\nannotation data block allows user interpre\xc2\xad\n\n4-11\n\n\nTable 4-3. The value of the latitude or\n\n4.3.2.3.2 Image Location Data Block\nThe image location data consist of 240 \n\nsixteen-bit words that describe the tick \n\nmarks that associate the scene with latitude \n\nand longitude. There can be a maximum of \n\nsix tick marks per side (i , left side, right \n\ne\nside, top and bottom), and the image location\ndata includes this tick mark information for\nRBV as well as MSS data.\n\nlongitude isgiven in degrees (3 characters)\n\nand minutes (2 characters).\nThere are two formats\nused to represent the\nlocation of tick marks. The tick marks are\nusually written first and are followed by the\nvalue of the latitude or longitude. If there\nis not enough room on any one of the sides\nfor the last tick mark, then the value of the\nlatitude or longitude is written first and is\nfollowed by the tick character for the last\ntick mark. An illustration of the two tick\nmark formats follows\n\nThe tick mark location data consist of four\ntypes: the tick position, the special tick\ncharacter, the direction (N, S, E or W),\nand the value in degrees and minutes. Each\ntick mark is denoted by a 16-bit s4gned\nbinary integer fraction that specifies its\nposition along the edge of the scene, followed\nby eight EBCDIC characters\nThe 16-bit signed integer fraction represents\nthe location of the tick mark along the edge\nof the scene and takes on values from +1/2 to\n-1/2. The most significant bit of the integer\nfraction indicates the sign of the fraction If\nthe bit is a one, the fraction is negative, if it is\na zero, the fraction is positive. The tick mark\nreference system has been chosen so that the\norigin is at the format center. The corners of\nthe scene writing area may be designated A\n(1/2, -1/2), B(-1/2, -1/2), C(1/2, 1/2) and\nD(-1/2, 1/2), as in Figure 4-18. The value\nthat locates the tick marks along the edges is\n\nFormat 1\nPosition.\n16-bit signed binary fraction\nTick mark annotation:\nTick mark character:\nX\'4F\' or\nX\'7E\'\nDirection, one character- N, 5, E or W\nValue\nDegrees, three characters.\nConstant. \'-\'\nMinutes, two characters 00 or 30\n\n4-17\n\n\nFormat \t2\n\npreceding the film image and 42 scan lines\nfollowing the film image). The tick marks\nare applied to the film image as shown in\nFigure 4-19.\n\nPosition- 16-bit signed binary fraction\nTick mark annotation.\nDirection, one character: N,\n\nS,\n\nE or\n\nW\n\nValue, six characters- same as Format 1\nTick mark character: X\'4F\' or X\'7E\'\n\nTICK MARK\n42 SCAN LINES\n\nEach of the eight tick mark tables (one forf\neach MSS and RBV edge) contains the tick\n\nCCTSCENE \t\n\nmark data arranged in positional order from\n\n2340SCAN LINES\n\nthe top of the \ttable downward with the top\nedge tick mark \ttable being given first.\n\n35SCANLINES\nFILM IMAGE\n\nI 42SCANLNES\n\nThe\n\nunused tick mark locations are signified by\nFigure 4-19. CCT and Film Image Comparison\n\na zero in the position words and X\'FF\' in\n\nall of the annotation characters.\n4.3.2.4 \tSpecial Image Annotation Tape\n(SLAT) Data File\n\nThe tick mark record format defined in the\n16-bit words is as follows:\n\nThis file consists of seven records. The first.\nrecord is a 2048 byte record which contains\nthe SlAT logical tape header. The second\nrecord contains 216 bytes of Processing\nInformation Data. The third record con\xc2\xad\ntains 204 bytes of Spacecraft and Sensor\nPerformance Data. The fourth record con\xc2\xad\ntains 144 bytes of Annotation Block Data\n(Table 4-5). The fifth record contains 76\nbytes of RBV Computational Data. base of over 6\nmillion images and photographs of the earth\'s\nsurface features, performs searches of specific\ngeographic areas of interest, and serves as a\nmgeraphiclareastofi\nnteresta e a\npr\nmanagement tool for the entire data repro\xc2\xad\nduction process. The computerized data\nstorage and retrieval system is based on a\ngeographic system of latitude and longitude,\nsupplemented by information about image\nquality, cloud cover, and type of data.\n\nThe success of the Landsat program has\ngenerated widespread interest in acquiring and\nusing Landsat data products. This section\nprovides the information needed to obtain\nthese products and describes the services and\nfacilities available to potential Landsat data\nusers.\nOnly those facilities that have been officially\ndesignated by NASA and/or other govern\xc2\xad\nment agencies as repositories of Landsat data\nare described,thus it is not an all-inclusive list.\nThe following facilities are discussed.\n(1) USGS Earth Resources Observation Systems (EROS) Data Center (EDC), (2) NOAA\nSatellite Data Services Branch (SDSB);\n(3) U.S. Department of Agriculture Aerial\n\nGuidance in the use of remotely.sensed data is\navailable at the EDC through scheduled train\xc2\xad\ning courses and workshops. The scientific\nteaching staff at the center periodically offers\ndiscipline-oriented courses in subjects such as\nagriculture, forestry, geography, geology, and\nhydrology. Visitors to the EDC can also\nPhotography Field Office (APFO), (4) NASAhyrlg.VststoheEOcnao\nreceive assistance in the operation of special\xc2\xad\nImage Processing Facility (IPF), and (5) the\nized equipment such as densitometers, addi\xc2\xad\nInformation Transfer Laboratory (Intralab) at\ntive color viewers, zoom transfer scopes, and\nthe Goddard Space Flight Center\nstereo viewers, and in the use of computerized\nmultispectral\nphenomena.\n\nLandsat data products may be purchased by\nanyone, including citizens of foreign countries, from the EEC, the SDSB or the APFO.\nThe IPF no longer supplies Landsat data\nproducts directly to users, but instead supplies second generation master negatives to\nthe three dispensing facilities,\n\nsystems to classify specific\n\nThe EROS Data Center also functions as an\nintegral part of the National Cartographic\nInformation Center (NCIC) system for those\nrequesting information about available air\xc2\xad\ncraft or space imagery and for those wanting\nto place orders for these data. The NCIC is\nheadquartered in the Geological Survey\nNational Center in Reston, Virginia. It ser\xc2\xad\nvices customers requiring information on the\navailability of cartographic data, including\nmulti-use mapping, geodetic control, areial\nphotography and space imagery. Qualified\npersonnel in the fields of topography,\ngeodesy, photogrammetry, photography and\ncartography provide help to those with spe\xc2\xad\ncialized needs. This service is readily available\nby a direct terminal link to the EDC\'s\ncomputer data base. Inquiries and orders for\ndata are transmitted daily from NCIC to the\nEDC to provide a timely response to customer\nneeds.\n\n5.2 EROS DATA CENTER\nThe EROS Data Center (EDC), at Sioux Falls,\nSouth Dakota, is operated by the U.S. Geological Survey EROS Program to provide\naccess to Landsat imagery, aerial photography\nacquired by the U.S. Department of the\nInterior, and photography and imagery\nacquired by NASA from research aircraft and\nfrom Skylab, Apollo, and Gemini spacecraft.\nThe primary functions of the EDC are data\nstorage, reproduction, dissemination and user\nassistance and training. The EDC and its\nprincipal facility, the Karl E. Mundt Federal\nBuilding, were dedicated August 7, 1973.\n\n5-I\n\n\nNCIC field offices with computer links are located at:\nTopographic Office\nU.S. Geological Survey\n900 Pine Street\nRolla, MO 65401\nPhone. 314-364-3680\nHours; 7.45-4:15\n\nAir Photo Sales\nU.S. Geological Survey\nFederal Center, Building #25\nDenver, CO 80225\nPhone: 303-234-2326\nHours: 7:45-4.15\n\nNCIC Information Unit\nNational Center - Stop 507\n12201 Sunrise Valley Drive\nReston, VA 22092\nPhone- 703-860-6045\nHours: 7:45-4:15\n\nMap and Air Photo Sales\nU.S. Geological Survey\n345 Middlefield Road\nMenlo Park, CA 94025\nPhone: 415-323-2157\nHours 7 45-8:15\nmaintain microfilm copies of Landsat data\navailable from the EDC and to provide assistance to the user in reviewing and ordering\ndata. These\nrequests can be made by mail, personal visit,\nor phone, either directly to the EROS Data\nCenter or to one of the EROS Applications\nAssistance Facilities. Users may request a\ngeographic search using any of three options:\n\nand white), acceptable degree of cloud cover,\nand acceptable quality. Most importantly,\nhowever, geographic areas must be clearly\nidentified and should be limited in size as\nmuch as possible to avoid a potentially long\ncomputer listing and the need to review large\nnumbers of choices. Latitude and longitude\ncoordinate specification is preferred, since\nthis is the method required for the computer\ngeographic search. A description of the in\xc2\xad\ntended application and use of the data will al\xc2\xad\nso assist the EDC, and may result in a more\nconcise response to the inquiry.\n\n1. \tPoint search - all images or photographs, any portion of which fall over\nthe designated geographic point, will be\nlisted.\n\nThe computer printout received as a result of\nthe geographic search lists all images available\nover or close to the user\'s specified area of\ninterest. With the printout, the user will also\nreceive detailed instructions on how to inter\xc2\xad\npret the printout and order the images\nselected, as well as an up-to-date price list.\nBriefly, the computer printout provides the\nfollowing information for each item listed:\ntype of coverage, type and size of master film\nsource, photo/scene identification number,\nindication of master film quality, percentage\nof cloud cover, date acquired, and scene\ncenter point (latitude/longitude). On the list\xc2\xad\ning, Landsat imagery is indexed and listed by\nindividual frame. Thus each entry on the\ncomputer listing describes a single image that\ncan be ordered directly by unique identifica\xc2\xad\ntion number. NASA aerial photography is\nindexed either by individual photograph or by\n\n2. \t Area rectangle (any area of interest\ndefined by four sets of corner coordinates in latitude and longitude) - all\nimages or photographs with any coverage of the area will be listed The area\nmust not exceed 200 one-degree\nsquares; e.g , 100 latitude by 200 longitude.\n3 \t Enclosed map (any point or area indicated) - all images or photographs\nmeeting the criteria for (1) or (2) will be\nlisted,\nWhen requesting a geographic search from the\nEDC, users should be sure to provide all\nrelevant information. This unit, a branch of\nthe Information Services Division of the\nNational Climatic Center (NCC), is located at\nthe World Weather Building, Suitland,\nMaryland. It has assumed the responsibility of\narchiving all environmental and earth resources data received from meteorological\nsatellites, Landsat and Skylab. It is collocated\nwith operational elements of the NESS.\n\nAlthough the SDSB does not presently have a\ngrographic search capability, NOAA has estab\xc2\xad\nlished 21 Browse Files throughout the U.S. to\nallow the general public wide access to infor\xc2\xad\nmation concerning Landsat imagery\nThe NOAA Browse Files consist of a 16 mm\nmicrofilm (and reader) of one channel of\nLandsat data, standard catalogs identifying\neach image, documentation giving additional\nLandsat system information, a list of products\navailable, prices of these products, and\ndetailed ordering procedures. All of the neces\xc2\xad\nsary material and information for the\nrequester to make a proper evaluation of the\ndata and subsequently order those products\nthat he requires can be obtained from the\nBrowse Files. Established in the mid-1930s, the APFO\nfilm library has files of Landsat and Skylab\nimagery in addition to the conventional ASCS\naerial photography and some black and white\nand color infrared photography from various\ngovernment agencies.\n\n1. \tDuplicate 70 x 70mm negatives\n2. \t Contact size 70 x 70mm or enlarged 9.5\nin. x 9.5 in. positive transparencies,\n3. \t Paper print enlargements in various sizes\nfrom 9.5 x 9.5 in. to 40 x 60 in.\n4. \t35mm slides\n\nAPFO receives a master file of 70mm nega\xc2\xad\ntives of all Landsat imagery from the NASA/\nGoddard Space Flight Center. In addition,\nAPFO has accumulated a library of over\n1,700 color composite negatives (MSS bands\n4, 5 and 7) of Landsat imagery. A computer\nlisting of color composite negatives on file is\nmaintained and may be sorted by scene\nidentification number, latitude and longitude\nand file number. Each scene also has a quality\nnumber ranging from 1-9 that indicates\nextent of scan lines, cloud cover, etc.\n\nLandsat data should be ordered, when possible, using the order blanks available at the\nBrowse Files listed in Section 5.3.1. However,\ndata products can also be ordered by letter\nrequest to the SDSB. Inquiries regarding\nproducts, ordering procedures and costs\nshould be directed to:\nSatellite Data Services Branch, D543\nNational Oceanic and Atmospheric\nAdministration\nWorld Weather Building, Room 606\nWashington, D.C. 20233\nTelephone- 301-763-8111/8112\n\n5.4.1 Browse File\nAs in the case of the SDSB, the APFO\nmaintains a Browse File to serve its users It\nhas also begun developing a geographic search\nand inquiry system- for the future. Currently,\nLandsat data computer query and search are\naccomplished by remote terminal from the\nMarshall Earth Resources Information Trans\xc2\xad\nfer System (MERITS) at Huntsville, Alabama.\nHowever, plans are being implemented where\xc2\xad\nby APFO will establish and maintain its own\nLandsat data retrieval system patterned after\nthe Marshall system.\n\nLandsat imagery must be requested by scene\nidentification number or by geographiical\ncoordinates, date and time. The requester\nshould also indicate the MSS or RBV channel\ndesired or, if unknown, state the use to be\nmade of the imagery to allow the SDSB\npersonnel servicing the request to select the\nmost advantageous channel. Standing order\nservice may be arranged by the user and the\nSDSB on an individual basis.\n\nThe APFO Browse File provides a microfilm\ncopy of its holdings plus catalogs, photo\xc2\xad\ngraphic indices and trained personnel to assist\nthe user in ordering data products\n\nBesides the photographic imagery provided by\nthe SDSB, copies of the original digital data\nreceived from Landsat are frequently\nrequested. To satisfy these requests, SDSB\nobtains the specified data on computer com\xc2\xad\npatible magnetic tapes from the EROS Data\nCenter and then reships them to the user. The\nSDSB plans to begin supplying CCTs directly\nto requesters in the near future.\n\n5.4.2 Product Order Placement\nLandsat imagery and NASA aircraft photo\xc2\xad\ngraphy may be ordered directly from the\n5-8\n\n\nAPFO. Inquiries regarding products, ordering\nprocedures and costs should be directed toU.S. Department of AgricultureAerial Photography Field Office\nAdministrative Services Division\n2505 Parley\'s Way\nSalt Lake City, Utah 84109\nTelephone: 801-524-5856\n\narchival or first generation positive film is\nstored on rolls at the IPF.\n\nASCS\n\nOf the services originally offered by the IPF,\nthe reception, processing, and distribution of\nData Collection System products are still\nhandled solely by the IPF The 70mm Browse\nFacility continues to provide the same range\nand quality of services to data users that it has\nfrom its inception The IPF sends computer\ncompatible tapes (CCTs) to the EROS Data\nCenter for reproduction, to be made available\nthere as requested by the public and the\ndispensing agencies\n\nReproductions of Landsat imagery are available in a variety of scales ranging from\n1:3,369,000 (70mm) to 1:250,000 (40 x 40\nin.) in black and white or color and on paper\nor film. ASCS aerial photography is also\navailable at the APFO as well as at county\nASCS offices\n\nReference should be made to Appendix H of\nthis handbook for a more detailed, inclusive\ndescription of the IPF.\n5.5.1 Brows Facility\n\nStanding orders for imagery may be established with the APFO on an individual basis.\n\nNASA provides a Browse Facility at the\nGoddard Space Flight Center (Building 23,\nRoom E408) where visitors may examine\narchived Landsat data, make use of their\nreference facilities, and view IPF output\nproducts.\n\n5.5 IMAGE PROCESSING FACILITY\nThe Image Processing Facility (IPF), formerly\nthe NASA Data Processing Facility (NDPF),\nbegan operations with the launch of the first\nof the earth resources satellites, Landsat 1, in\nJuly of 1972. From that time until approximately the launch of Landsat 2 in January of\n1975, the Support (User) Services Section of\nthe IPF functioned as th6 primary souree of\ndata for NASA investigators and user\nagencies Since the launch of Landsat 2,\nhowever, the role of the IPF has been\nsignificantly altered. With the exception of\ncertain instances where federal government\nagencies require occasional data on a fast\nturn-around basis (e.g., disaster assessment) or\nfor some operational requirement, the IPF\nsupplies Landsat data only to the three\nabove-mentioned federal data centers As the\nsingle U.S. source for processing video telemetry data from Landsat, the IPF also sends\nsecond generation master negatives of all\nprocessed Landsat imagery to these agencies\non a daily basis. Products are then generated\nfrom these negatives, at the agencies, for sale\nat nominal prices to the public. The original\n\nA full-time Browse Facility assistant is avail\xc2\xad\nable to instruct and assist visitors in the\ncapabilities and operational aspects of the\nfacility The assistant will place orders for\ncatalogs or microfilm for investigators desir\xc2\xad\ning retention copies and will instruct and aid\ninvestigators in the use of the IPF information\nsystems data base search and query system.\nThe assistant is also available to demonstrate\nthe use of catalogs, atlases and user guides A\nlog of all reference material is maintained in\nthe Browse Facility and is available to visitors.\nThe assistant will provide instruction for the\noperation of light tables, microfilm viewing\nequipment, and the cathode ray tube (CRT)\nterminal\nInformation about every image processed\nfrom Landsat video data tapes is maintained\nin the IPF information systems data base, and\nincludes the following parameters:\n\n5--9\n\n\no \t Observation ID\n\n* Sun Elevation\n\n* Orbit No\n\n* Sun Azimuth\n\n* Station ID\n\n* \t Quality (good, fair, poor)\n\n* Ephemeris Type (Best fit or predicted)\n\n* \t Cloud Cover (percent)\n\n* Transmission Mode (Direct or Recorded)\n\n* Geographic Area\n\n* Altitude\n\no Time\n\n* \t Heading\n\n* Sensor\n\n* Track\n\n* \t Image Product (type and spectral band)\n\nTo permit investigators to conveniently search\nthis data base, a special computer program\nknown as Query Processing is available. It can\nbe used either in a batch mode or in an\ninteractive mode directly from the remote\nCRT terminal in the Browse Facility. The\nprogram is such that a search can be made for\ngiven time periods, given geographical areas or\nimagery parameter information. Moreover,\nthe program is sufficiently flexible to allow\nalmost any logical combination of search\ncriteria to be specified.\n\nbe obtained directly through the IPF, DCS\ndata are still available\nFor those desiring DCS data, it may be\nrequested on a standing order basis or on a\nData (Variable) Request basis. All orders must\nspecify product type and platform number,\nand will be processed on an individual basis\nby IPF Support Services personnel. Users may\ndiscuss DCS data products with representa\xc2\xad\ntives who are trained to assist them in\nformatting their requests Contact may be by\nphone, mail or personal visit to the following\naddress:\n\nThe \tnormal output of a search is the number\nof images found that meet the specified\nsearch criteria. Intralab provides an opportunity for\nusers to acquire hands-on experience in the\napplication of remote sensing technology to\nproblems of immediate concern to their\noperational needs. Users work with Intralab\ndiscipline specialists and contemporary image\nprocessing and analysis facilities in conducting\nshort duration projects.\n\n3. \t A display image output that is a printout of all data in the data base for each\nimage that satisfies the search criteria,\n(See Figure 5-2.)\n5.5.2 Product Order Placement\nAlthough Landsat imagery and CCTs can not\n\n5-10\n\n\nKEY TOIMAGE CLARITY\nG = GOOD\nF = FAIR\nP = POOR\nTHE IMAGES INDICATED BELOW ARE ON\nMICROFILM ROLL NUMBER 001\n\nOBSERV ID ORB\n\nTRANS\nMODE\nR M\n\nSTAT TOT\nR M CC EPH\n\n1010 05435\n1010 05442\n1010 05444\n1010 05451\n1010 05453\n1010 05480\n1010 05462\n1010\n05465\n1010 05471\n1010 05474\n1015 08000\n1015 08002\n1016 08030\n1010 08032\n1016 08035\n\nD\nD\nD\n0\n0\n0\n0\nD\nD\nD\nD\n0\nD\n0\n0\n\nOL\nGL\nFA\nFA\nFA\nGD\n3D\nGD\nD\nGD\nGL\nGL\nFA\nFA\nFA\n\n0173\n0173\n01850\n0185\n0185\n0196\n0196\n0196\n0196\n0196\n0173\n0173\n0185\n0185\n0185\n\nD\n0\nB\nD\nD\nD\nD\nD\nD\nD\n0\n0\nD\n\nGL\nGL\nFA\nFA\nFA\nGD\nGD\nGD\nGO\nGO\nGL\nGL\nFA\nFA\nFA\n\n21\n32\n11\n31\n32\n51\n11\n21\n11\n32\n41\n42\n41\n41\n42\n\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\nR\n\nQUALITY\nRBV MSS\nPROD 12312345\n10\n11\n20\n17\n18\n13\n14\n15\n16\n12\n10\n11\n20\n17\n18\n\nPICTURE CENTERSUBSTPOINT\nLONG\nLAT\nLONG\nLAT\n\nGGGGOGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGGGGG\nGGGGOGG\nGGCGGGG\nGGGGGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGOGGGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGGGGGG\nGGGGGG6\nGGGGGGG\n\n12055\n121 02\n14812\n14828\n14836\n8084\n810\n81 07\n8108\n8112\n12055\n12102\n14812\n14828\n.14836\n\n3021 -12056\n3493 -121 12\n6303 .14822\n6209\n14833\n6008 14836\n4179\n8083\n4075\n8103\n3958\n81 06\n3847\n8107\n3740\n8109\n3621\n12056\n34.93 12112\n6303 14822\n6200 14833\n6090 14836\n\n3632\n3512\n6303\n6200\n6098\n4183\n4076\n3863\n3851\n3742\n3632\n3512\n6300\n6200\n0098\n\nObservation Identifier J\n\nSubiatellne Point\n\nOrbit Number\n\np\nChastty\n\nMSS\nTransmion Mode for REV (RIand (M)\nD - Be consistent with available Intralab\nresources\n\n5.7.1 Landsat Standard Catalogs\nAs part of its support services function, the\nIPF produces two standard Landsat catalogs:\nthe U S. Standard Catalog (listing all data\nprocessed by the IPF of the U.S. and parts of\nCanada and Mexico), and the Non-U.S Stan\xc2\xad\ndard Catalog (listing data processed by the\nIPF over all other areas of the world).\nCumulative U.S Standard and Non-U.S. Stan\xc2\xad\ndard Catalogs are published on ayearly basis.\nCatalogs for Landsat 1 are available for the\nperiods of launch to July 1973, July 1973 to\nJuly 1974, and July 1974 to July 1975. For\nLandsat 2 the publishing schedule will be\nfrom launch to January 1976, January 1976\nto January 1977, etc. To supplement the\ncumulative catalogs, monthly catalogs are\nissued listing the data processed during the\nmonth of issue.\n\n2 \t Active user participation in project\ndesign and performance isessential:\na. Users assign personnel to work with\nthe Intralab staff\nb. Users collect the necessary surface\nobservations and/or supporting data\nc. Intralab acquires and supplies remotely sensed data\nd. Data analysis and interpretation are\nconducted by a joint user/Intralab\nteam\n\nEach of the standard catalogs also contains an\noutline map. The U.S. Standard Catalog has\noutline maps of the United States, including\nAlaska and Hawaii (see Figure 5-3), and the\nNon-U.S. Standard Catalog contains an out\xc2\xad\nline map of the world (see Figure 5-4). Each\nof these maps graphically depicts the areas\ncovered by the images listed in the catalog. In\naddition, the U.S. outline, map shows an\nestimate of the cloud cover contained in the\nimagery by a four-shade spectrum along the\nsubsatellite path for each north to south pass.\nNo shading indicates that no imagery was\ncollected.\n\n3. \tCompleted tasks are fully documented\nby the user/Intralab teams, with particular attention to:\na. Techniques commensurate with the\nuser\'s operational requirements\nb. Cost effectiveness of the techniques\nc. Identification \tof the characteristics of\nremote sensing systems that more\nfully satisfy user requirements\n4. \tIntralab recognizes a responsibility to\nshare these experiences with broader\nuser communities having similar infor\xc2\xad\nmation needs. All documentation ispublically available, and is disseminated\nthrough appropriate NASA and other\noffices,\n\nA large part of each standard catalog consists\nof computer listings produced from the IPF\ninformation system data base. All listings are\nin sequence by observation identifier.
