Annotated Sample of Standard Catalog\n\n5-14\n\nA typical roll of microfilm contains the\nfollowing\xc2\xad\n\n5.7.2 Microfilm\nOnce a month, in conjunction with prepara\xc2\xad\ntion of the standard catalogs, the IPF produces 16mm microfilm (MSS, band 5) depicting imagery processed during the month.\nThese images are intended only to indicate to\nusers what imagery is available and are not\nsuitable for data analysis.\n\n1. \t 1 Leader Frame of Microfilm Identifica\xc2\xad\ntion\n2. \t Up to 1000 Frames of RBV Images\n(where available)\n3. \t Upto 1000 Frames of MSS Images\n\nThe microfilm data is also divided into U S.\nand non-U.S. segments Each set of microfilm\nimages is in exact correspondence to the\nstandard catalogs and can be used in conjunction with the catalog for selecting desired\nimages. The catalog contains roll and position\nnumbers and data which, along with the\nmicrofilm image, provide the user with\nenough information to decide whether or not\nthe image is useful,\n\nThe microfilm is on open reels suitable for\nmounting by the user in a cartridge. Two\nrapid search capabilities are incorporated on\nthe microfilm. The first allows counting\nimages for precise location and requires a\ncounting capability on the viewer being used.\nThe second is a moving bar indicator that\npermits gross location of images to within\nabout 20 images Details of these two schemes\nare described in the standard catalogs\n\nBecause the microfilm images are intended to\nprovide only a summary of the data available,\nthe images are limited to one band each for\nthe RBV (if available) or MSS. Only the RBV\nband 2 images and the MSS band 5 images are\nreproduced.\n\nThe RBV and MSS image frames per observa\xc2\xad\ntion are alternated on the roll of microfilm as\nshown in Figure 5-7.\nA complete set of microfilm data is main\xc2\xad\ntained in the IPF Browse Facility and com\xc2\xad\nplete sets are sent both to the EDC and\nAPFO. Users may purchase copies of the\nmicrofilm sets through EDC User Services.\n\nEach image is a photograph of a system\ncorrected image and contains the image\nidentifier and full annotation block as described in Section 4.\n\n18 mm \t\n\nMIROML\nID\n\nIMG\n\nGMS]E\nIMAGE\n\nMAGE \t\t\nG\n\n2\n\nLANOSAT OBSERVATION NO.\n1\n\nFigure 5-7. Com\xc2\xad\npensation for these distortions is prowded\nduring ground image processing in the NASA\nImage Processing Facility (IPF) by applying\ncorrection factors for the measured attitude\nrates.\n\nThe Landsat 1 and 2 Observatories (Figure\nA-i) are earth-pointing stabilized spacecraft\nconsisting of integrated subsystems that provide the power, environment, orbit maintenance, attitude control, and information flow\nrequired to support the payloads for a period\nof at least one year in orbit. They weigh\napproximately 953 kilograms (2100 Ib) and\nhave an approximate overall height of 3\nmeters (10 ft) and a diameter of 1.5 meters\n(5 ft), with solar paddles extending out to a\ntotal of 4 meters (13 ft).\n\nThe 3-axis active ACS uses horizon scanners\nfor roll and pitch attitude error sensing. The\nrate gyros sense yaw rate and, in a gyro\ncompassing mode, sense yaw attitude. A\ntorquing system uses a combination of reac\xc2\xad\ntion jets to provide spacecraft momentum\ncontrol and large control torques when re\xc2\xad\nquired; flywheels are utilized for fine control\nand residual momentum storage.\n\nSOLAtRPADDLE\nASSEMBLY\n\nA.2 ATTITUDE MEASUREMENT SENSOR\nANTENNA\n\nThe Attitude Measurement Sensor (AMS) is\nan independent component (not used for\n\nAnTREEE\nT L\n\nTAECOER\n\nHORIZSCANNR\n\nFattitude\n\nELETRONICS\n\ncontrol purposes) that determines\n\nprecise spacecraft pitch and roll attitude. This\n\nTANK\n\nis used for image location and correction\n\n,data\n\n-YAXS(H\n\n(ROLL)\n\n, OBITu\nSENSORY\n\nduring ground processing. The AMS detects\nthe radiation level change in the 14 to 16\nmicron range between the earth\'s atmosphere\nand the spatial background and establishes the\npitch and roll axis positions relative\nto the local vertical. After ground compensa\xc2\xad\ntion of telemetry data for variations due to\n\nNO0ZZLE\nANTENNA\nWA\n\nANITENI\n|\nDATA COLL\n\n"spacecraft\nION\n\nWDESANDANTEdNA\n2\nUNIFIEDAND\nNEY\n\nDATA\nCLTWO\n\nMULTISPECThAL\nS ANNER\n\nRETRNBEAMA\nVIDHeIC\nCAMERAS\n\n+ZAXIS(VAW)\n\nATTITUDE\nMEASUREMNT\nSESOR\n\nseasonal-radiance and other effects, the pitch\nand roll attitude can be determined to within\n\nFigure A-1. Landsat 1and 2 Observatory\n\nabout 0.07 degree.\n\nA.1 ATTITUDE CONTROL SUBSYSTEM\n\nA.3 WIDEBAND VIDEO TAPE\nRECORDER SUBSYSTEM\n\nThe Attitude Control Subsystem (ACS) provides spacecraft alignment with both local\nearth-vertical and orbit velocity vectors, and\nprovides rate control about the pitch, roll and\nyaw axes. The ACS achieves pointing accuracies of the spacecraft axes within 0.4 degree\nof the local vertical (about the pitch and roll\naxes) and within 0 6 degree of the velocity\nvector (about the yaw axis). The rotation\nrates encountered\nduring multispectral\nscanner (MSS) and return beam widicon\n(RBV) operations are less than 0.015 degree\nper second about all axes These rates produce\n\nTwo wideband video tape recorders (WBVTR)\nrecord, store, and reproduce the data outputs\nfrom either the RBV or MSS during remote\nsensing operations Each recorder can record\n30 minutes of either 3.2-MHz video analog\ndata from the RBV or 15-Mbps digital data\nfrom the MSS. Data are recorded by four\nheads (on one wheel) rotating across the\n2-inch wide tape. Recording and playback are\neach at 30 centimeters per second (12 in /sec)\nand are in the same direction. Total usable\ntape length is 548 meters (1800 ft) for each\nrecorder.\nA-I\n\nThe RBV analog video signal is transformed\ninto the FM domain by video circuitry in the\nWBVTR. The signal is received as a negative\nanalog signal, is dc level shifted, frequency\nmodulated, amplified and recorded. To insure\nhead switching during the horizontal blanking\ninterval of the video signal during playback,\nthe RBV signal is rephased to the WBVTR\nheadwheel at the beginning of each triplet\nexposure during recording. In playback, the\nRBV signal is read out sequentially by the\nsame four rotating heads, with appropriate\nswitching, producing a continuous RBV signal\nin the FM domain. The signal is then demodu\xc2\xad\nlated on the ground, producing the original\nanalog video waveform,\n\noperation and 521 Watts for remote opera\xc2\xad\ntion Considering the subsystem as an energy\nbalanced system, it can support an average of\n20 minutes of payload (both RBV and MSS)\n"ON - time" per orbit initially and 12.1\nminutes after one year. The reduction in "ON\n- time" is mainly due to efficiency loss of the\nsolar arrays from small particle impact during\nthe year in orbit. However, the actual payload\n"ON - time" is limited by other system\nconstraints (such as station pass time, record\ncapability, etc.) to an average of 12 minutes\nper orbit\nAll power is provided from the batteries\nduring the launch phase and while the space\xc2\xad\ncraft is within the earth\'s umbra. Energy from\nthe solar array not required for spacecraft\nloads during the lighted periods is used to\nrecharge the batteries and any excess power is\ndumped via auxiliary loads.\n\nThe MSS digital video data is received as a\nnon-return to zero level (NRZ-L), 15-Mbps\ndata stream. In the WBVTR, the data stream\nis re-clocked and then frequency modulates\nan FM carrier. The resulting frequency-shift\nkeyed (FSK) signal is recorded by four\nrotating heads. The MSS data are recorded\nasynchronously, that is, the data stream and\nrotating heads are not synchronized. In playback, the MSS signal is read out sequentially\nby the same four rotating heads, with switching and demodulation producing a continuous\nNRZ-L, 15 Mbps data stream.\n\nA.5 \tCOMMUNICATIONS AND DATA\nHANDLING SUBSYSTEM\nThe Communications and Data Handling Sub\xc2\xad\nsystem (Figure A-2) provides for all spacecraft\ninformation flow and is composed of the\nWideband Telemetry Subsystem and the nar\xc2\xad\nrowband Telemetry, Tracking and Command\nSubsystem.\n\nEach WBVTR can record and playback either\nRBV or MSS data at any given time. The\nselection of RBV data or MSS data for each\nWBVTR during record or playback, plus\nappropriate tape motion to select the proper\ntape location, is made by appropriate ground\ncommands which can be stored by spacecraft\nequipment for subsequent remote execution\n\nA\'\n-1\n\nA.4 \t POWER SUBSYSTEM\n\nPAYLOAD S-BAND\n\nThe Power Subsystem supplies the electrical\npower required to operate all spacecraft service and payload subsystems During sunlight\n\n,tk\n\nperiods the subsystem delivers a maximum\noutput of 980 Watts of regulated -24 Volts\nfor short periods. Analog data has 8-bit accuracy\nor 1 part in 256.\n\nWideband Telemetry Subsystem accepts\nprocesses data from the RBV, the MSS,\nboth wideband video tape recorders,\ntransmits it to the ground receiving sites,\n\nThe subsystem consists of two, 20-Watt\nS-band FM transmitters and associated filters,\nantennas, and signal conditioning equipment.\nAs shown in Figure A-3 the subsystem permits transmission of any two data sources\nsimultaneously, either real time or recorded,\nover either of the two downlinks (one data\nsource each). Commandable power level\ntraveling wave tube (TWT) amplifiers and\nshaped beam antennas provide maximum\nfidelity of the sensor data at minimum power.\nCross-strapping and dual mode operation (two\ndata sources) with a single TWT amplifier is\navailable in the event of hardware malffunctions\n\nnnRB\nINPT\n\nSIGNAL\nER\nCONDITION\n\nREALTME\nWTH I\n\nVCOA\n\nVIDEO\nOR\nLI\nSIG AL\n\nThe USB equipment has the capability to\ntransmit on separate subcarriers real-time\ntelemetry (768 kHz), playback data (597\nkHz), DCS data (1.024 MHz) and pseudo\xc2\xad\nrandom ranging information simultaneously\nover the same 2,287 5 MHz carrier. In addition it\nprovides the link for transmitting the Data\nCollection System (DCS) data.\n\nCommanding can be performed via either the\nVHF link at 154.20 MHz or by the USB link\nat 2,106.4 MHz into redundant sets of\nreceivers on the spacecraft. These commands\ncan be any of the 512 possible commands\nexecutable by the command/clock or any of\n\nA-3\n\n\nthe 8 commands recognizable by the cornmand integrator unit. A total of 30 cornmand/clock commands can be "stored" for\nexecution outside of the range of the ground\nstations. All remote payload operations are\nperformed using stored commands.\n\nlishes the precise Landsat orbital parameters\nafter orbit insertion and makes adjustments\nthroughout the life of the mission to maintain\noverlapping coverage of sensor imagery and\nlong-term repeatability.\nThe OAS is a monopropellant, hydrazine\xc2\xad\nfueled, propulsion system constructed as a\nsingle module consisting of three rocket en\xc2\xad\ngines, a propellant tank and feed system, a\nsupport structure and the necessary inter\xc2\xad\nconnect plumbing and electrical harnessing\nThe OAS is mounted to the spacecraft\nsensory ring with a thruster located along\neach \tof the (+) roll, (-) roll, and (-) pitch axes,\nsuch that each thrust vector passes approx \xc2\xad\nmately through the spacecraft center of mass.\nWith these thrust vectors, the orbit adjust sub\xc2\xad\nsystem can impart incremental velocities to\nthe spacecraft to correct in-plane injection\nerrors, inclination injection errors, and orbit\nperturbations due to atmospheric drag and\nother error sources over an orbital life of\nmore than one year.\n\nA.6 \t THERMAL CONTROL SUBSYSTEM\nThe \tThermal Control Subsystem provides a\ncontrolled environment of 20 \xc2\xb1 100 C for\nspacecraft and sensor components Thermal\ncontrol is accomplished by both semipassive\n(shutters and heaters) and passive (radiators,\ninsulation, and coatings) elements Shutters\nare located on each of the peripheral compartments on the sensory ring, and are actuated\nby two-phase, fluid-filled bellows assemblies.\nThese assemblies are clamped tightly to heat\ndissipating components and position the shutter blades to the proper heat-rejection level,\nHeaters are bonded at various locations in the\nsensory ring to prevent temperatures from\nfalling below minimum levels during extended\nperiods of low equipment-duty cycles. The\nheaters are energized selectively by ground\ncommand when the temperature level at these\nlocations falls below a predetermined value.\nThe \t upper and lower surfaces of the sensory\n\nA.8 \t ELECTRICAL INTERFACE\n\nSUBSYSTEM\n\nThe \tElectrical Interface Subsystem functions\ninclude power switching, telemetry signal\ngeneration, switching logic, power fusing, data\nrouting, time-code processing and automatic\n"shut-off" of equipment. Timers associated\nwith the payloads, WBVTR and S-Band Trans\xc2\xad\nmitter are provided to automatically remove\npower after 32 minutes of operation if the\nnormal turn-off does not occur. Power switch\xc2\xad\ning (regulated and unregulated), transient load\ncircuitry, and fusing are included in this sub\xc2\xad\nsystem for the RBV, MSS, WBVTRs and the\nOAS.\n\nPassive radiators coated with a low-absorptivity, high-emissivity finish are used to assist\nthe shutters in rejecting the heat from the\nsensory ring. Spectral Response for Landsat\n\nMSS Bands\nMULTISPECTRAL SCANNER SUBSYSTEM\nBand\n\nC.1 INTRODUCTION\n\n4\n5\n6\n7\n8\n\nThe multispectral scanner subsystem (MSS) is\na sensor system that produces a continuous\nstrip image of the earth in various spectral\nbands. The Landsat 1 and 2 MSS responds\nto earth reflected sunlight in four spectral\nbands; the Landsat-C MSS will carry an\nadditional band responding to thermal infra\xc2\xad\nred radiation.\nThe MSS continually scans\nthe earth in a 185.2 km (100 nm) swath\nperpendicular to the Landsat orbital track,\nScanning is accomplished in the crosstrack\ndirection by an oscillating mirror, satellite\nmotion along the orbit provides the alongtrack scan.\n\nSpectral Response\n\n(Micrometers)\n\n0.5-0.6\n\n0.6-0.7\n\n0.7-0.8\n\n0.8-1.1\n\n10.4- 12.6 (Landsat-C only)\n\nEach MSS spectral band in Landsat 1 and 2\nutilizes six detectors. Photomultipliers are\nused in bands 4, 5 and 6. Band 7 uses silicon\nphotodiodes to achieve extended infrared\nresponse. Band 8 will employ two mercury\xc2\xad\ncadmium-telluride detectors.\nWith the exception of the band 8 detectors,\nall other detectors are coupled to the focal\nplane of the MSS optical system by means of\nsquare optical light pipes. The six light pipes\ncorresponding to a given spectral band con\xc2\xad\nduct the radiance at the focal plane to iden\xc2\xad\ntical optical bandpass filters immediately\npreceding each detector. It is essential to\nunderstand that data in the various spectral\nbands are acquired sequentially (within 65\nmicroseconds for Landsat 1 and 2) and not\ninstantaneously.\nFor Landsat-C, sequential\nacquisition of all 5 bands occurs in 800 micro\xc2\xad\nseconds\n\nThe analog signals produced by the MSS\ndetectors are digitized and formatted into a\n15 megabit data stream for transmission to an\nearth receiving station or for on-board recording. During subsequent signal processing by\nthe NASA Image Processing Facility (IPF),\nthe MSS data are transformed into framed\nimagery with a 10% overlap between frames\nand an area coverage approximately registered\nwith that produced by the return beam\nvidicon (RBV) framing cameras. Conversion\nof continuous strip MSS data to corresponding framed imagery is accomplished by\nutilizing the known instant of RBV exposure\nand satellite position as provided by spacecraft clock signals and tracking station posi\xc2\xad\ntion data.\n\nC3 SCANNING GEOMETRY\nThe MSS scanning geometry is illustrated in\nFigures C-1 and C-2.\n\nC.2 SPECTRAL RESPONSE\nThe nominal instantaneous field of view\n(IFOV) of each detector in bands 4, 5, 6, and\n7 is 79 meters square as determined by the\nfocal length of the telescope, the nominal\naltitude of the spacecraft and the dimensions\nof the light pipes at the focal plane. The\nIFOV of each band 8 detector is 237 meters\nsquare at nominal altitude. As the mirror\n\nMSS spectral response for Landsat 1 and 2\nand Landsat-C are shown in Table C-1. Note\nthat bands 1, 2 and 3 designate the RBV subsystem spectral response bands. Landsat-C\nnomenclature may require alteration due to a\ncontemplated reduction in the number of\nRBV cameras from three to two.\n\nC-1\n\n\nAs the mirror scans west to east, the six scan\nlines of bands 4 through 7 and the two lines\nof band 8 correspond to an along-track swath\ndistance of 474 meters (6 x 79 or 2 x 237).\nFor the next swath to be contiguous to the\npreceding swath, the spacecraft must move in\nthe along-track direction by 474 meters in\nSatellite nadir point\none mirror period.\nvelocity must then be 6.456 km/sec\n(474 +0.07342) or 3.484 nm/sec. Telescope\nfocal length, spacecraft altitude, light pipe\ndimensions, spacecraft velocity and mirror\nperiod were chosen to achieve the cross-track\n\nscans the earth from west to east, the resulting image is seen by each bank of detectors in turn. As a result, six scan lines of\nvideo information are produced in bands\n4, 5, 6 and 7 and two in band 8. The nominal\nmirror frequency is 13.62 Hz corresponding\nto a period of 73.42 milliseconds. The mirror\nscan and retrace periods are each 36.71\nmilliseconds. The time during which the\nearth scene is acquired in the west to east\nscans is 33.0 milliseconds. This avoids acquiring data during nonlinear motion intervals when mirror velocity is reversed,\n\ncoverage described above.\n\n-contiguous\n\nMINLLYAT\n\n2 FOTA 1\nBUD\n\nThe\n\ncatadioptric telescopes employed in Landsat\nand 2 have focal lengths of 82.09 cm\n(32.32 inches) and 82.3 cm (32.4 inches)\n\nSCAN\nMIROR\nO IN LT\nM L Y\n\nDCAR\nER B N EN\nA\n\nrespectively.\nDuring every mirror retrace period, the\nby a mechanical\nradiance from the earth scene is blanked out\nThe individual\nsensors in bands shutter.\n\nPIT"\nVIEW-I\nFIELDOF 1SOEGREES\n\nWEST EAST\nTO\n\n4 through 7\nare exposed\n\nevery other mirror retrace to a rotating\ndensity\nwedge\noptical\nfilter\nilluminated by on-board calibration lamps.\nBand 8 detectors are exposed to tempera\xc2\xad\nture references during alternate mirror re\xc2\xad\n\nAvariable\nNORTH\nWEST J\n\n,\n\nSPACECAFT \n\nSOUTH\n\ntraces when bands 4 through 7 are not being\ncalibrated. Landsat I and 2 Light Pipe Array\n\nand Detector Sampling Sequence\nband. Band 8 detectors are labeled A and\nB. Band 8 detectors are coupled to the telescope focal plane via germanium relay optics,\nthe entrance pupil of which is also shown in\nFigure C-4. The fight pipe array dimensions,\nphysical arrangement and sampling process\nare directly related to mirror velocity and\nspacecraft motion. An understanding of this\nrelationship is essential to the calculation of\neffective field of view as well as in understanding computer compatible tape (CCT)\nproduction.\n\nmicrosecond .\nEach detector represents a\nchannel of data and 25 such channels exist\non the Landsat spacecraft (24 detectors plus\none channel for multiplexed thermal band\ndata).\nEach detector analog output is en\xc2\xad\ncoded as a six bit digital word, each word\ncorresponding to one picture element (pixel).\nThe word period is then 0.3983 microsecond\n(6 x 0.0664) which is the sampling time per\ndetector.\nBecause there are 25 channels,\neach detector (with the exception of band 8)\nis sampled once in every 9.958 microseconds.\n\nThe MSS mirror is driven at a frequency of\n13.62 Hz derived by counting down the frequency of a crystal-controlled clock using a\ncount-down factor of 135x 213 = 1,105,920.\nThus the 13.62 Hz is derived from a frequency of 15.0626 MHz. This frequency is\nsignificant in that it represents the maximum\nbit rate that can be accommodated during\ndetector sampling. Time\' per bit is 0.0664\n\nConsider a ground scene composed of a single\n79 x 79 meter object, imaged on Detector A,\nband 4 at a time t = 0. The active scan time\nduring which video is acquired is 33 milliseconds.\nIn the west to east active scan\nperiod, two auxiliary sensors determine the\nmirror angular position and initiate and\nterminate detector sampling. The two sensors\ninsure that the cross-track optical scan is\n\nC-3\n\n\n185.2 km regardless of mirror scan nonlinearity or other perturbations of mirror\nvelocity. Cross-track image velocity isnominally 5.612 meters per microsecond. After\n9.958 microseconds, the 79 x 79 meter\nimage has moved 55.99 meters. The S1\nsample taken at this instant represents 23\nmeters of previous information and 56 meters\nof new information. Therefore, in practice\nthe "effective" IFOV of a detector in the\ncross-track direction must be considered to\nbe 56 meters corresponding to a nominal\npixel area of 56 x 79 meters (at nadir point).\nUse of the "effective" IFOV in area calculation therefore eliminates the overlap in area\nbetween adjacent samples (pixels).\n\nprecedes band 7 data by 44.612 micro\xc2\xad\nseconds and band 6 precedes band 7 by\n20.314 microseconds.\nThis spatial mis\xc2\xad\nregistration is corrected by inserting the\nappropriate number of dummy bytes prior\nto the data in bands 4, 5 and 6 during CCT\nproduction.\nIn using the term "byte", it\nis important to distinguish between "space\xc2\xad\ncraft" bytes and in-band bytes. For example,\nin band 4, detector A is sampled once every\n9.958 microseconds so that one in-band byte\nof information is created in this time period.\nThe number of bytes generated in all bands\nin this period is 25. Thenumber of in-band\nbytes required to achieve spatial registration\nis illustrated in Figure C-4. Bands 4, 5, 6\nand 7 are offset from each other by two\nin-band bytes.\n\nUpon the completion of two complete\nsampling intervals, an elapsed time of 19.916\nmicroseconds, plus one sequential sample\ntime of 0.3983 microsecond, detector A of\nband 5 is sampled.\n\nM\n\nIn 20.314 microseconds\n\nA\n\nthe image of the ground has moved 114\nmeters. The 25th channel\nC--A\n\n\n-\n\nin Landsat 1 and 2 carries a signal known as\nminor frame synchronization (MNFS) and\nits digital complement (MNFS).\nThese\ndigital codes are used during ground data demultiplexing to tag the beginning of the\nspacecraft sampling sequence and thereby\nkeep track of the identification of each data\nbyte. In addition, provision is made\nfor linear amplification or non-linear amplifi\xc2\xad\ncation, which is selectable by ground issued\nspacecraft commands.\n\nEach sequence (for example, MNFS, 1 ... 24)\nrepresents 25 samples (spacecraft bytes) in a\nperiod of 9.958 microseconds. Note that\ndetector A of band 8, is interrogated every\n75th sample. Similarly, detector B of band 8\nis interrogated every 75th sample. Bands 4\nthrough 7 theoretically contain 3,314\nsamples/detector. That is, there are 3,314\nperiods of 9.958 microseconds duration in\nthe 33 millisecond acquisition time (active\nportion of the mirror scan period). Band 8,\ndetectors A and B, due to the reduced\nsampling rate, produce a maximum of 1,104\nsamples each within the 33 millisecond active\nmirror scan.\n\nSignal compression, via four-segment quasi\xc2\xad\nlogarithmic amplifiers, is generally employed\nto improve the signal-to-noise ratio in bands\n4, 5 and 6. By compressing high radiance\nlevel signals, the quantization noise more\nnearly matches photomultiplier noise. Band\n7 signals, derived from silicon photodiodes\nare never compressed because equivalent\nload resistor noise is best matched by linear\nquantization. The available ground com\xc2\xad\nmandable analog processing options are\nillustrated in Figure C-5. In the high gain\nmode applied to bands 4 and 5, amplifier\ngain is increased by a factor of 4. This\nallows greater use of system dynamic range\nfor those scenes producing low sensor\nirradiance.\n\nIn some instances users will desire approximate registration of band 8 data with data\nfrom bands 4 through 7. The user can\nachieve the desired registration by the\nappropriate insertion of dummy bytes in\nbands 4 through 7 as follows. At t = 0,\nassume a 79 x 79 meter ground patch is\ncoincident with the IFOV of detector A in\nband 4. MSS Tape Data Format From FR 1928 (One Track - Typical)\n\nc-7/8\n\n=\n\nAPPENDIX D\n\nMSS COMPUTER COMPATIBLE TAPE\n\n\nSubsystem that generates CCT products of\nselected MSS scenes for the user community\n\nD.1 INTRODUCTION\nMSS data are acquired at a real-time rate of\napproximately 600 kilobits/second per chan\xc2\xad\nnel on an Ampex FR1928 multi-track recorder. Conventional computer systems and\nperipherals operate in the range of 0.25 to 1\nmegabit/second. Computer compatible tape\n(CCT) products must therefore be recorded\nat substantially lower bit rates. This is accomplished by means of an off-line process in\norder to maintain the IPF information handling capacity at the 600 kilobats/channel rate\nfor the generation of photographic film products.\n\nIn the original IPF configuration, MSS video\ntapes were reformatted and recorded on a\nNewell HDTR. This recorder and associated\ndata reformatting equipment have been super\xc2\xad\nseded by Ampex FR2014, 14-track recorders\nand new formatting and interface electronics\nthat produce the exact equivalent of the\nNewell HDT. A block diagram of the pre\xc2\xad\nvious and present configuration is illustrated\ninFigure D-1.\n\nThe Newell HDT format consists of four data\ntracks that are simultaneously accessible Ln\nall, 16 tracks are available, four at a time\nEach track, ina group of four, represents one\nspectral band of MSS data (for Landsat 1 and\n2). Scan lines are in a sequential (series) for\xc2\xad\nmat on each track. For Landsat-C, quarter\nscan lines of band 8 are interleaved after\nevery three full scan lines in each track The\nserial scan line format permits simultaneous\n\nAs a first step in the off-line process, the\nMSS data are reproduced at the 600 kilobit/\nchannel rate, reformatted and recorded on a\nhigh density digital tape recorder (HDTR).\nThe resulting high density digital tape\n(HDT) is then reproduced at a substantially\nlower tape speed to achieve the necessary bitrate reduction for CCT generation. This HOT\nis the principal data input to the IPF Digital\n\nFORIDRIF CONFIGURATION\n\nMIGH \n\nPIICETLOR \n\n\nOSIITY\nRGWR)\n\nMELL\nHUT\n\n\nI.DIA\n\nItnAPE\n\nP\nP REPROCESSED\n\nFR2014\n\nSGYTM\n\nI&EW\nCGIIFJUR\nATION\n\nI\n\nIENIT\n\nI\n\nI\n\nt.NKTCO\n\nDIGITAL\n\nG i\n\nATA\nV\'.D.T\n\nIN\n\nEL\' E.\n1\nN011T IINANES\n\nV1/4\n\'IA\'IL\nI\n\nIRA \xc2\xa5 S\nI..\n\n///////\nAD\n\n/ll.\n/lN /\n\nFigure D-1 Previous and Current Equipment Used to Produce Computer Compatible Tape Products\n\nD-I\n\n\naccess to picture elements in each of the four\nspectral bands as required to implement the\nspectral interleaving format of CCTs.\n\nD.2 NEWELL HDT FORMAT STRUCTURE\nTo assist in understanding the production of\nCCTs, the Newell HDT format, now simulated\nby the FR2014 recorder and DSI as seen by\nthe Digital Subsystem, is described.\n\nThe Newell HDT format differs from the\nFR1928 format in a number of additional as\xc2\xad\npects. During the reformatting process, spacecraft time code is removed. In place of time\ncode, a frame identification (Frame ID) is\ninserted at the beginning of each frame of\ndata to uniquely identify the center point of\nthe image and the time of imaging. Time\ncode per se is no longer of interest because\nthe "framing" of the MSS continuous strip\nimage is performed in response to a specific\nuser request. Each group of scan lines is also\npreceded by the appropriate six words of calibration coefficients and line length corrections prior to processing of the scan line data\nAll\' pixels remain encoded as six-bit bytes\n(whether acquired in compressed, linear, high\nor low gain modes). Decompression (if required), calibration and line length correction\nremain to be accomplished within the Digital\nSubsystem. As recorded, the HDT MSS data\nalso retain the data as acquired with spatial\nmisregistration.\n\nAn MSS scene isdefined as an area of 185.2 x\n185.2 km (100 x 100 nm) represented by\nfour spectral bands of video data for Landsat\n1 and 2 and by five bands of data for LandsatC In digital terminology, spectral band and\nframe have become synonymous. The Newell\nHDT, or its equivalent, generally contains up\nto five scenes. Each spectral band or frame of\nthe scene is made up of 390 successive space\xc2\xad\ncraft mirror sweeps. Each mirror sweep re\xc2\xad\nsuits in six scan lines of video per spectral\nband (Landsat 1 and 2) Therefore, each\nband or frame contains 2340 scan lines (390 x\n6) For Landsat-C, bands 4, 5, 6 and 7 each\ncontain 2340 scan lines. Band 8, using two\ndetectors, is represented by two scan lines per\nmirror scan. Therefore, 780 scan lines of\nband 8 represent a 185.2 x 185.2 km area.\n\nPreviously, the spacecraft scan mirror fre\xc2\xad\nquency was stated to be 13.620 Hz/second.\nNevertheless, the angular velocity of the mir\xc2\xad\nror is subject to perturbations during the ac\xc2\xad\ntive scan period. This period wasestablished\nby two angular position sensors that initiate\nand preempt detector sampling. The result\nis a slight variation in the number of bytes\n(samples or pixels) per scan line. Landsat 1\nperformance typically results in 3,216 \xc2\xb1L 6 and\nLandsat 2 in 3,247 + 5 bytes/scan line The\ndigital systems within the IPF can accommo\xc2\xad\ndate up to 3800 bytes per band 4 through 7\nscan line\n\nThe Digital Preprocessor Subsystem (DPPS),\n-FR2014 recorders and Digital Subsystem Interface (DSI) produce an output to the Digital\nSubsystem identical to that produced by the\nNewell HDT; however, the DPPS permits extensive quality control and data screening not\npossible in the earlier subsystem\nFor example, detection of substandard scan line data\nautomatically results in substitution of the\nprevious scan line "Quick-look" assessment\nof digital imagery is also provided by means\nof a cathode-ray tube display and storage tube\nfor image retention. Although the DPPS re\xc2\xad\nmoves spatial misregistration and retains time\ncode for high speed tape search, the output\nsignals from the DSI are again spatially deregistered and stripped of time code to match\nthe Newell format.\n\nFor illustrative purposes, 3220 pixels (bytes\nor samples) will be chosen for use in the\nfollowing HDT format discussion. Each band\n8 detector, having one-third the resolution of\nband 4 through 7 detectors, will generate\n\nD-2\n\n\nFigure D-2A pertains to any scan hne in\nbands 4 through 7 of Landsat 1 or 2. Figure\nD-28 details the format for a band 8 scan\nline.\n\n1073 bytes per scan line Band 8 data are to\nbe added into each track of the four track\nHDT in the form of one quarter scan line\n(QSL) which will therefore be composed of\n268 bytes. The digital equipment was design\xc2\xad\ned to accept up to 300 bytes per QSL Fig\xc2\xad\nures D-2A & D-2B show the encoding of one\nscan line of video data. If calibration data\nwere acquired during the mirror scan that is\nassociated with that scan line, six of the calibration wedge bytes precede the scan line If\nno calibration was present, six bytes of all\nzeros are substituted (Landsaf 1 and 2) It is\nimportant to appreciate that each video scan\nline was generated from a specific spacecraft\ndetector. During prelaunch calibration, six\nbytes of calibration wedge data were uniquely\nselected for that detector Therefore, the six\nbytes of calibration data preceding the scan\nline on the HUT are unique to that scan line\n(and subsequent scan lines derived from the\nsame detector), however, when a defective\nscan line is replaced by the DPPS, incorrect\ncalibration of that scan line will occur. Calibration bytes associated with band 8 are also\nlisted as six bytes; however, only two levels\ncorresponding to two temperatures, that of\nthe cold (self-look) reference and the hot\n(shutter housing) reference, will be obtained,\n\nThe complexity of the scan line format can be\nexplained as follows. Preamble A is present\nto permit byte synchronization prior to ac\xc2\xad\nquisition of line synchronization (LS). Line\nsynchronizations 1, 2 and 3 establish that cali\xc2\xad\nbration data are about to be presented fol\xc2\xad\nlowed by line length code LLC. Gap A pro\xc2\xad\nvides a time period during which computa\xc2\xad\ntions can be performed to arrive at radio\xc2\xad\nmetric calibration gain and offset coefficients\nas well as a computation of the number of\nbytes to be permitted in the next scan line to\nPreamble B again establishes\nbe processed\nbyte synchronization and LS 1, 2 and 3 are\nprecursors of data Using the value of 3220\nbytes per scan line and the maximum design\nvalue of 3600 picture elements, there will be\n3220 bytes of video followed by 380 bytes of\nfill code. (In practice, the number of bytes\nper scan line on the HDT is a variable that is\nto be subsequently given a fixed value during\nCCT generation). For\nLandsat 1 and 2 the total number of bytes per\nscan line, with necessary computer synchron\xc2\xad\nizing codes and gaps, amounts to 7490 bytes/\ntrack (where four tracks represent the four\nspectral bands). For Landsat-C, band 8 video\nis multiplexed into the four data tracks,\nAfter every three full scan lines (bands 4\nthrough 7), a quarter scan line of band 8\nvideo is inserted. Because the resolution of\nthe band 8 detectors is one-third that of detectors in bands 4 through 7, approximately\n1073 samples (per detector) were acquired\nduring one mirror scan. A QSL therefore contains about 268 bytes. These 268 bytes of\nvideo are followed by 32 bytes of fill. Again,\nthe number of actual video bytes will vary\nwith mirror velocity, as indicated by the line\nlength code and will be set to a fixed value/\nmirror scan during CCT generation Detector\nA and detector B video for band 8 are alternated. That is, the first QSL will represent\ndetector A data. After three full scan lines of\nbands 4 through 7 data, a QSL of detector B\ndata in the format shown in Figure D-2B will\nbe present. Each QSL of band 8 format will\ncontain exactly 2106 bytes.\n7490 +\n\nBYTESITRACK\n\n7490 +\n\n7400 +\n\nFigure D-4 depicts the HDT scene format.\nEach scene is preceded by a scene gap code\nthat establishes synchronization of the com\xc2\xad\nputer at the bit level Scene synchronization\nestablishes synchronization at the byte levelScene ID identifies the image in terms of the\ntime of acquisition measured from the day of\nspacecraft launch, in days since launch, hours,\nminutes and seconds, the spacecraft is also id\xc2\xad\nentified.\n\nThe 185.2 x 185.2 kilometer scene required\n390 spacecraft mirror scans. Each mirror\nscan generated six scan lines in each of the\nfour spectral bands, bands 4 through 7. Each\nof these scan lines is encoded as 7490 bytes.\nIn band 8, two scan lines are produced per\nmirror scan. One quarter scan line is encoded\nas 2106 bytes. Therefore one track of video\ndata on the HDT contains (390 x 6 x 7490 +\n390 x 2 x 2106) = 19,169,280 bytes\n(exactly).\n210\n\nf\n\n7490 +\n\n7490 +\n\n7490 +\n\n2186\n\nTRACK 1\n\nSCAN\nLINE 1\n4\nBAND\n\nSCAN\nLINE 2\nBAND4\n\nSCAN\nLINE 3\nBAND 4\n\nOSL\n1ST\nBET A\nBAND\n8\n\nSCAN\nLINE 4\nBAND 4\n\nSCAN\nLINE 5\nBAND 4\n\nSCAN\nLINE 6\nBAND\n4\n\n1ST\nOSL\n\nGETB\n\nBAND 8\n\n\nTRACK 2\n\nSCAN\nLINE I\n5\nBAND\n\nSCAN\nLINE 2\nBAND S\n\nSCAN\nLINE 3\nBAND 5\n\n2NDOSL\nDETA\n8\nBAND\n\nSCAN\nLINE 4\nBAND S\n\nSCAN\nLINE S\nBAND 5\n\nSCAN\nLINE S\nBAND\n5\n\n2ND\nOSL\nDET B\nBAND 8\n\nTRACK3\n\nSCAN\n1\nLINE\nBAND 6\n\nSCAN\nLINE 2\nBAND 6\n\nSCAN\nLINE 3\nBAND S\n\n3RDO\nSL\nDETA\nBAND8\n\nSCAN\nLINE 4\nBAND 6\n\nSCAN\nLINE S\nBAND 6\n\nSCAN\nLINE 6\nBANDS\n\n3RD DSL\nDET B\nSAND 8\n\nTRACK 4\n\nSCAN\nLINE 1\nBAND 7\n\nSCAN\nLINE 2\nBAND 7\n\nSCAN\nLINE\n3\nBAND 7\n\n4TH OSL\nBET A\nBAND S\n\nSCAN\nLINE 4\nBAND7\n\nSCAN\nLINE S\nBAND7\n\nSCAN\nLINE\n6\nBAND7\n\n4TH QSL\nBET B\nBAND\n8\n\nNOTES\n\nISL = QUARTER SCAN LINE, 1BYTE = 6 BITS = I PIXEL\nLINE FORMAT\nSEE\nFIGURES 0 2A, D2BFOR SCAN\n\nFigureD-3. Detection and printout of processing\nerrors for CCT quality control\n\nD.3 DIGITAL SUBSYSTEM\nThe Digital Subsystem processes selected MSS\nscene data into computer compatible tape\n(CCT) form. The MSS data input is either the\nNewell HDT or its equivalent from the MSS\npreprocessor (MPP) FR2014 as modified to\nthe Newell format by the Digital Subsystem\nInterface Unit. Input bit rate is 250,000 bits/\nsecond/track, which is accomplished by reducing tape playback speed relative to recording speed.\n\nA block diagram of the Digital Subsystem is\nshown in Figure D-5 In addition to the MSS\nvideo input, other inputs include an auxiliary\npaper tape, punched cards, teletype instruc\xc2\xad\ntions and an eight-track image annotation\ntape. Output consists of four computer cornplatible tapes per four-band input scene. Each\noutput tape represents a 25 x 100 nautical\nmile area of the imaged scene and contains all\nspectral data associated with that image seg\xc2\xad\nment.\n\nThe Digital Subsystem accomplishes the fol\xc2\xad\nlowing:\n\nThe auxiliary paper tape informs the Digital\n\n1. During previous pro\xc2\xad\ncessing of the scene by the Initial Image Gen\xc2\xad\n\nD-6\n\n\neration Subsystem, a determination was made\nof the greatest number of bytes associated\nwith a scene scan line This number, Nmax ,\nwhich is supplied by the auxiliary paper tape\ninput, is used as follows\n\nThe initial deltas must be adjusted to main\xc2\xad\ntain spectral registration Let\nAb = 14- 2b\nwhere b is the spectral band number (4, 5, 6\nor 7) Then\n\nNmax = Number of bytes in the longest\nscan line\n\nLLC\nCLL\n\nAinitial = A - Ab\n\n= Number of bytes in the scan line\nto be processed\n= Corrected line length\n\nThe synthetic byte is inserted by repeating\nthe preceding byte. Because the byte count,\nas determined by the mirror period, remains\nconstant over many months, comparisons of\nmulti-temporal data in user change detection\nprocessing will seldom if ever exhibit the\npresence of synthetic bytes.\n\nThe corrected line length must be a multiple\nof eight because the output CCT format contains two bytes each from bands 4 through 7\ninterleaved as eight-byte groups. The CLL\nmust also be a multiple of three because band\n8 data have one-third the resolution of bands\n4 through 7 data, and a quarter scan line of\nband 8 data is to be multiplexed into the out\xc2\xad\nput tape after every three full scan lines of\nband 7inteleaed dta~the\n4 hrouh\nbands 4 through 7 interleaved data (across\nfour tapes). Therefore the CLL must be a\nmultiple of 24.\n\nD.3.3 Decompression\nta inverse acquie spacecraftcompress md is\nare of the in t\ncompression\napplied to both video and calibration wedge\nsignals before calibration\nRegardless of\nwhether the data were acquired in linear or\ncompressed form, one byte contains six bits.\n\ncompression is accoplihedin the Dital\nDecompression is accomplished in the Digital\n\nAfter insertion of six dummy bytes, to restore\nband-to-band registration, the number of\n6.\nbyts i th\nloges scn lne s Nax\nbytes in the longest scan line is Nmax + 6.\n\nSubsystem computer by means of a table\n\nThe CLL is expressed as 24 times the largest\nintegerL\n\nis output as 0-127. MSS Full Scene Interleaved Record Format\n\nis then 3n. CCT number 1 contains groups 1\n\nD.5\n\nthrough 3n. CCT number 2 contains groups\n3n + 1 through 6n, etc. All of these groups\nare associated with scan line "k" Continuing\nthis process for all scan lines results in the\nwestern-most 25 nautical mile north-to-south\nimage segment being represented by CCT\nnumber 1. CCT number 4 represents the\neastern-most 25 nautical mile strip. Strip\nlength extends 100 nautical miles north and\nsouth.\n\nADDITION OF BAND 8 TO THE CCT\nFORMAT\n\nBand 8 video and calibration are spatially\nregistered and added as independent records.\nThey are not spectrally interleaved with band\n4 through 7 data. In a complete MSS scene of\n390 mirror sweeps, 780 scan lines of band 8,\n390 for detector A and 390 for detector B,\nare generated. These scan lines are adjusted\nto 24n - 3 = 8n video byte lengths. One\nquarter scan line, or 2n bytes, is added to\neach CCT after every three quarter-scan lines\nof interleaved spectral data from bands 4, 5,\n6 and 7. The first band 8 record is from\ndetector A. After three quarter-scan lines of\ninterleaved spectral data from bands 4, 5, 6\nand 7, a second band 8 record, representing\nthe output of detector B, is added. Each\nband 8 record consists of 2n bytes of video\nplus 14 bytes of calibration data per CCT.\n\nThe quarter scan line of four-band spectrally\ninterleaved data plus four-band calibration\ndata contains 24n video bytes and 56 calibration bytes. This data string is designated as a\ntape record Ri, k "I" denotes the image segment (same as CCT number) and "k" designates the scan line number. Every record is\nseparated from every other record by an\ninter-record gap (IRG).\n\nD-1I\n\n\nFormat of the calibration data is the same as\nfor the other bands; however, it is expected\nthat there will be only two calibration word\nvalues repeated three times to fill the 6 bytes\nallocated for six calibration words. A typical\n\nband 8 record, either detector A or B, is\nillustrated in Figure D-9. "k" is the scan\nline. The satellite is at a\nnominal altitude of 920 kilometers (570\nmiles). The DCPs operate\ncontinuously, sampling the sensors period\xc2\xad\n\nThe DCP is connected to individual environmental sensors that are selected and provided by the investigator or user agency to\nsatisfy their own particular needs Up to eight\nindividual sensors may be connected to a\nsingle DCP. Precise control of the orbital parameters\nis required to achieve and maintain the\ndesired characteristics. Hence, the Landsat\nspacecraft include an orbit adjust capacity\nthat is used to attain the orbit initially and\nmaintain this orbit throughout the life of the\nmission.\n\nF.1.1.2 Inclination Errors\nInjection inclination errors cause adrift in the\ntime of the descending node and also imagery\nsidelap errors. Without an orbit adjustment\ncapability, the injection inclination error\nwould result in a continually increasing side\xc2\xad\nlap error. These inclination effects can be\ncompensated for by adjusting the orbital\nperiod.\n\nThe Orbit Adjust Subsystem is a monopropellant system consisting of three rocket\nengines fed by a common propellant/pressurant tank. The three thrusters are aligned to\nprovide impulse along or opposed to the\nspacecraft velocity vector and also perpendicular to the orbital plane. Each thruster imparts a thrust of approximately one pound\n\nF.1.1.3 Error Correction\nThus, injection period errors had to be re\xc2\xad\nmoved and compensation provided for the\ninclination error. Period adjustments are\naccomplished by utilizing one of the two\nthrusters which impart impulse along the\nvelocity vector. Because of the one pound\nforce of these thrusters, the weight of the\nspacecraft, the magnitude of the period\nadjustment, and other scheduling criteria, the\nperiod adjustment process took several days\nfrom injection to completion.\n\nF.1.1 Attainment of Required Orbit\nThe Delta launch vehicle injects the spacecraft\ninto its final orbit to within the limits of the\nerrors inherent in the launch vehicle system.\nLaunch vehicle errors at injection are random\nand can be of magnitudes that impact the\ndesired observation characteristics. The spacecraft orbit adjust capabilities are utilized after\nspacecraft separation to remove residual\nlaunch vehicle injection errors.\n\nF.1.2 Maintenance of Required Orbit\nSeveral forces (such as. atmospheric drag, the\ngravitational attraction of the sun and moon,\nand the spacecraft\'s own attitude control\nmass explusion subsystem) act upon the\nspacecraft after the desired orbit has been\nattained. These forces cause changes to the\norbit that compromise the desired coverage\nand repeatability characteristics. The orbit to\nwhich the injection error removal process was\ntargeted had been selected to minimize the\neffects of these forces on the desired coverage\ncharacteristics. Nonetheless, orbit adjustment\nis occasionally required to compensate for\nthese forces.\nDuring the first several weeks of the mission,\nseveral small although significant perturbing\nfactors (e.g., the force due to the attitude\n\nThe orbital parameters most critical to providing the desired imagery characteristics are\nthe semi-major axis (or equivalently the\nperiod of the orbit), the inclination, and\nthe eccentricity. For Landsat, a unique combination of orbital period and inclination is\nrequired to establish the desired coverage\npattern and sun synchronization. Errors in\neccentricity also affect these characteristics,\nHowever, eccentricity errors have a negligible\neffect compared to the effects of inclination\nand period errors.\nF.I.1.1 Period Errors\nThe maximum expected injection error in the\norbital period exceeds by a wide margin the\nF-I\n\n\ncontrol system mass expulsion subsystem)\nwere determined. These factors were then\nincluded in orbit planning operations to minimize the number of subsequent adjustments.\nSeveral small adjustments were necessary\nduring this period to optimize the desired\ncoverage characteristics. These adjustments\nwere minor and were scheduled so as not to\ninterfere with imaging operations.\n\n11\n\n10\n\nORBIT\nADJUST\n\nORBIT\n\nNO 4\n7\n\n9\n\nADJUSTORIT\nNO 1S\n+7\n\n7 A5 \n\n\nw\n\nORBIT\n\nORBIT\n\nADJUST\nNO 6\n\nADJUST\nNO 7\n\n20\n\nPERIOD OF HIGH\n3O O\n\nSEAST\n\n+653\n\n+291\n\n1\n2\n\n05>\n\nACS PITCH GATE\n\n,\n\n.\n\n2\n\nACS\nGAS DUMP\n\n+685\n\n0\n\nS00>4\n\nO WEST\n\nSubsequent to the first several weeks of the\nmission, the requirements for adjustments\nbecame minimal, systematic, and predictable.\nThe requirements for these adjustments result\nfrom the perturbing forces on the spacecraft\nwhich, over long periods of time, cause\npredictable perturbations to the orbit. The combined effects of\noblateness and perturbing forces will cause\nthe altitude of the satellite to vary periodi\xc2\xad\ncally within the range of 880 to 930 km\nthroughout the mission life.\n\nimages formatted 25 seconds apart. The orbit\nis maintained such that no more than 37\nkilometers cross-track picture-center variation\nwill occur over the mission life. The in-track\nscheduling will assure that no more than 30\nkilometers in-track picture-center variation\nwill occur.\nWith two satellites operating simultaneously,\nthe relative phasing of their orbits is nominally established to offset the 18-day repeat\npattern of one spacecraft by 9 days with\nrespect to the other. In this way, the space\xc2\xad\ncraft overfly a given area every 9 days. By\nmaintaining both flight orbits to a common\nnominal repeat-orbit pattern, and by scheduling the operation of each spacecraft with\nreference to its equator-crossing point,\nimagery from the two satellites is framed\nconsistently and a 9-day repeat coverage\npattern is effectively established. To alleviate\ncommunications overlays, Landsat 1 will be\nmoved, during the period October 1976 to\nFebruary 1977, to a new orbit schedule\n6 days behind Landsat 2, giving successive\nrepeat cycles of 6 and 12 days.\n\nF.2.5 Mean Sun Time\nThe Landsat orbit is sun-synchronous, as\nshown in Figure F-8; hence, the geometric\nrelationship between the orbit\'s descending\nnode (southbound equatorial crossing) and\nthe mean sun\'s projection into the equatorial\nplane will remain nearly constant throughout\nthe mission. As a result, the mean sun time at\neach individual point in the orbit will remain\nfixed and, in fact, all points at a given latitude\non descending passes will have the same mean\nsun time. For Landsat\'s 1 and 2 the mean sun\ntimes of the descending node were initially\nestablished at launch as 9 42 and 9 32 a.m.,\nrespectively.\nF-8\n\n\nLandsat. All spacecraft and operations scheduling, commanding\nand spacecraft related data evaluation for the\nLandsat missions are controlled by the OCC.\nIts 24-hour-a-day activities are geared to the\noperational timeline dictated by the orbit and\nground station coverage capabilities. The\nmajor elements of the OCC are shown in\nFigure F-18.\n\n-\n\nF.3.1 System Scheduling\nAt the beginning of each spacecraft day the\nactivity plans for that day are generated by\nthe OCC for each orbit\'s operation, based on\nsensor coverage requirements, spacecraft and\npayload status, network availability and the\ncurrent cloud-cover predictions. Priorities are\nassigned to coverage requirements for select\xc2\xad\n\ning the data to be collected over various\ngeographic locations as described in Section\nF.4. Sensor operations, including real-time\nand remote coverage, and calibrations are\nscheduled. Current spacecraft and payload\nstatus are examined to ensure effective utilization of observatory capabilities. Tracking and\norbit adjust requirements are obtained from\nthe NASA Orbit Determination Group when\nrequired and are integrated with coverage\nplanning. Scheduling is coordinated with the\nnetwork operations center and station avail\xc2\xad\nability is determined for both routine contact\noperations and orbit-adjust maneuvers. After\nintegration of all the required data sources\nand support activities, a final activity plan is\nissued. This plan is the integrated, time\xc2\xad\nordered sequence of events defining the space\xc2\xad\ncraft, payload, and ground system operations\nfor each orbit, and serves as the basis for the\ncompilation of spacecraft command lists.\n\n-SPACECRAFT\n\nANL\n\nREMOTE\nIA\n\nGROUN A\n\nSIGMA\n3\n\nT\n\n,\n\nTERMNG\nINALS\n\nD T\n\nS\n\nROP\n\nA\nT\n\nC\n0\n\nPERFORMANCE\n\nARLE\n\nT M\n\nO\n\nMU 1\n\nCOMMUNI\nCATIONSGO\n\nB\n\nATAOTAPES\nONS\nTOIF\n\nPROCESSOIRS\n\nBC\n\nSITES\nO\n\nCOMMANDS\n\nHTrIF\n\nU\nT\nN9\nSTAPES\n\nDCS\n\nPAYLOADVIDEO\n\nNTTF\n\nDATA CONTROLAND DISPLAYS\n\n-n-FSTATUS\n\nS1..\nVIDEOTAPES\n\nPAYLOAD\n\nI:-\n\n00\n\n0\n\n000\n\n0\n\n00\n\n0\n\n0\n\n0\n\n000\n\nFigur F- M ajo Elmet of Opeaon Conro Center\nKEYBOARD\n\nKEYBOARD\n\nKEYBOARD\n\nMWSRBVVIDEO\n\n--\n\nOCC\n\nT\nTAPES OIPF\n\nFigure F-18. Major Elements of Operationscontrol Center\nF-44\n\nKEYBOARD\n\nCONSOLES\nOPERATIONIS\n\n_J\n\nKEYBOARD\n\nF.3.2 Data Acquisition\n\nF4 MISSION PLANNING\n\nAfter acquisition of telemetry signals from\nthe spacecraft, the narrowband housekeeping\ndata (real time and playback) and Data\nCollection System (DCS) information are\nrouted via the NASA Communication\n(NASCOM) network to the OCC. The realtime spacecraft data are then displayed on\nfive operations consoles where computerdriven status lights and CRT displays provide\nthe spacecraft evaluators with a complete\non-line determination of vehicle and payload\nstatus, performance, and health, as well as\ncommand verification\nDCS data are also\nprocessed in the OCC on-pass and placed on\nmagnetic tape. These tapes are available\nimmediately post-pass for continued process\xc2\xad\ning in the NASA Image Processing Facility\n(IPF),\n\nEach spacecraft has access to all global areas\nbetween 81 degrees north and 81 degrees\nsouth latitude every 18 days. However, due to\nconstraining factors both within and external\nto the Landsat system, not all of this area can\nbe imaged all the time. The constraints\ninclude:\n1. On-board tape recorder capacity of 30\nraxpmum\ninu t\nminutes maximum\n\nIn-depth spacecraft evaluation and image\'\nannotation information are derived from the\ndata stored on the narrowband tape recorders,\nThese data contain all the satellite telemetry\nfor the entire orbit, including all remote areas.\nPlayback data are received during the station\npost-pass to produce detailed spacecraft\nevaluation parameters and trends. The Spacecraft Performance Data Tape is also generated\nfrom this playback data and given to the IPF\nfor use in generation of image annotation\nparameters.\nVideo data received during Network Test and\nTraining Facility (NTTF) station passes are\nrelayed directly to the OCC where they are\nprocessed in the identical manner as at other\nremote sites as outlined in Appendix D. The\nvideo tapes generated in the OCC are handcarried to the IPF for image processing.\n\nThe purpose of the mission planning function\nis \t to define the sequence of spacecraft and\nground-station operations to maximize the\nimagery yield while operating within these\nconstraints. The output is a time-ordered\nsequence of events that define all sensor,\nwideband tape recorder, and assorted routine\nspacecraft functions. This sequence of events\nis then used to define the specific commands\nfor operating the spacecraft. In addition, the\nmission planning function defines the events\nthat are to occur during every spacecraft/\nground station contact.\nThe bulk of the mission planning operation is\ndone once a day and results in activity plans\nfor that day\'s operation. These plans are\nupdated on an orbit-by-orbit basis as required\nto account for any last-minute anomalous\noccurrences such as ground station outages.\n\nF.3.3 Command Generation\n\nFigure\n\nThe Landsat spacecraft are commanded by an\noperator at either of two operations consoles\nlocated in the OCC. All commands are\nchecked and then routed by the OCC computer system to the remote receiving site that\nis in contact with the satellite. At the site, an\n"as transmitted" command check is performed and command acknowledgements are\nrelayed back to the OCC. Final command\nverification is made through analysis of telemetry data\n\ntypical day\'s operation for Landsat. Each\nspacecraft will normally be scheduled to send\nreal-time (direct) data whenever it is concur\xc2\xad\nrently over an area of interest and is within\nview of a ground station that can receive\nLandsat data. The three primary Landsat\nstations shown in Figure F-3 (NTTF, Gold\xc2\xad\nstone, and Alaska) provide coverage of most\nof North America and real-time imagery\ntransmission is normally scheduled during this\ntime. Data recovery over the rest of the globe\n\n2. \t On-board command memory capability\nfor switching sensors on and off\n3. \t Ground station availability and contact\ntime duration\n\n5. \t Ground scene illumination conditions\n6. \t Cloud cover\n\nF-45\n\n\nF-3 illustrates the coverage for a\n\nDuring remote operations the spacecraft has\naccess to much more coverage area than can\nbe accommodated by the wideband tape\nrecorders. Therefore, a selection process is\nrequired to determine which areas are to be\nrecorded during any given remote operation.\nTo assist in this selection process, a system of\npriorities is used for all coverage areas of the\nworld. By scheduling payload operations\nbased on these priorities, coverage of the areas\nof greatest investigator interest isassured.\n\nProject to assist in defining the various areas\nof interest and priorities.\nPredicted cloud cover data is used in mission\nplanning to minimize the number of obscured\nimages. Prediction data is received from the\nNational Oceanographic & Atmospheric\nAdministration (NOAA) on a periodic basis\nand the spacecraft schedule is updated as near\nas possible to the upcoming data pass to\ninclude the most recent cloud information.\nDue to spacecraft command constraints, the\nsensors and recorders can be switched on and\noff only a limited number of times; hence,\nsome imagery of fully cloud-covered areas\nmay be taken.\n\nIn order to establish the priorities, several\nfactors must be considered. These include:\n1. Scientific importance of the area- is\nthere investigator interest in a given area\nand how often need it be imaged7\n2. \t Season of the year - when is imagery of\nthat area most/least desirable?\n3. \t varies with conditionsLighting scene contrast image quality\nand brightness\nvwih\ncn e ontaraeswth bi l ns\nn\n\nThe decision not to schedule sensor operation\nover a given area depends both on the\npercentage of cloud cover expected and the\ndegree of investigator interest in that partic\xc2\xad\nular area. Areas of very high investigator\ninterest are normally scheduled even though a\nfairly high percentage of cloud cover is\npredicted. Areas of low, or no investigator\ninterest tend not to cloud cover. The objec\xc2\xada\nlesser percentage of be scheduled even for\ntive is to maximize the number of cloud-free\n\nis performed by recording the data on the\non-board wideband video tape recorders and\nplaying back during subsequent ground\nstation contacts.\n\nwhich in turn varies with\n\nlocal sun\n\nimages while at the same time making every\nattempt to image the areas of greatest in\xc2\xad\nterest.\n\nangle; what lighting conditions are required for the given scene?\n4. Time \t since the area was last imaged \xc2\xad\nhow recent is the imagery for that area;\nwas it obscured by clouds?\n\nThe possibility of cloud obscured scenes has\none major implication to investigators who\nrequire periodic repeating coverage. Since the\nsatellites have access to a given scene only\nonce every 9 days (except for higher lati\xc2\xad\ntudes), cloud cover could result in the repeat\ncoverage being interrupted for periods of 18,\n27, or more days for any particular scene.\n\nThe priorities in the system are quite dynamic\nin that they must be periodically updated to\nreflect changes in the desirability of imaging\nthe various areas. Spacecraft/Ground\nCommunication Links\nG-2\n\nX\n\nG.2 \t PAYLOAD WIDEBAND\nCOMMUNICATIONS\n\nG2.2. \t Ground Receiving and\n\nRecordings\n\n\nG.2.1 \t Spacecraft-to-Ground Communication\n\nFigure \t G-3 illustrates the flow of the wide\xc2\xad\nband data as they are received and recorded\nAt the Alaska and Goldstone stations the data\nare received and demodulated and then hard\xc2\xad\nwired into special Remote Site Equipment\nwhere they are processed and recorded. For\nthe NTTF station at GSFC, the Receiving Site\nEquipment is physically located in the Opera\xc2\xad\ntions Control Center rather than at the sta\xc2\xad\ntion. This permits operations personnel to\ndirectly monitor the display equipment during\ndata reception from NTTF\n\nRBV and MSS wideband data are normally\ntelemetered simultaneously to one of the\nthree prime ground stations over two S-band\nlinksoperating at center frequencies of 2229.5\nMHz and 2265.5 MHz. The RBV camera has a\nvideo bandwidth of 3 2 MHz and is used to\nfrequency modulate the carrier within an RF\nbandwidth of 20 MHz The MSS output is a\nsingle Pulse Code Modulation-Non-Return to\nZero Level (PCM-NRZL) encoded bit stream\nat a bit rate of 15 06 Mbps This PCM signal\nFrequency Shift Key (FSK) modulates the\ncarrier\n\nThe Receiving Site Equipment for the RBV\nincludes equipment to resynchronize and re\xc2\xad\nclamp the video, a video display CRT, and\nvarious test equipment This equipment moni\xc2\xad\ntors the data as they are received and supplies\nthe necessary timing and control signals to the\nvideo tape recorder The recorder records the\ncomposite video signal on tape for physical\ntransfer to the IPF\n\nBoth RF links contribute a small degradation\nto the data. For the RBV the degradation in\nsignal-to-noise ratio is less then 1 dB. The\nMSS bit error rate is less than 1 in 105. These\nare worst-case values expected at the 20\nelevation limit of the three primary ground\nstation viewing cones\n\nD, L V\n\n\nASKA\nS\nOLO O NEA\t\nEO\nMSVJOD\n\nRNVAND\nSEKSORS\nFROM\nSPACEBORNE\n\nR\n\n\'WADEBAND \n\n\nI\n\nSPACEBORN\n\nFROO\n\nTAE\nRECOSES\n\nTAPES\n\n\nSYNCHRONIZER\n\nDEMODDULATOR\n\nSITE EQUIP\n\nOCC\n\n\nNTTF\n\nFROMSFACEBDRNE SENSORS\nI\n\nDIVlE11\n\nRO\n\nIA D\n\nRB\n\nLIN\n\nMARRLED\nTO;PF\n\n\nDISPLAY\n\nI\n\n\nNTTF \n\n\nRECORDER\n\nRECEIVING\nE QI\n\nnS\nIOR\n\nDM D T\n\nTAPE\nRECORDER\n\nTAPES\n\n\nI \n\n\nHAND\n\n\nRCVYR \n\n\nCARRIED\n\n\nLINE\n\nFigur 6-.\nIS\nN\n\nEAS\n\nldbad\n\n"\nido\nF\n \t\n\nat Fo\n\nSYNCHROINZR\n\nORIVERDEMODULATOR\n\n,\'\n\n\nFigure G-3. Wideband Video Data Flow\nG-3\n\n\nE\n={ RECI VING ]\nST\n\nQI\n\nTAPE;\n\nRECORDER\n\nThe Receiving Site Equipment for the MSS\ndemultiplexes the serial bit stream intoindi\xc2\xad\nvidual data channels corresponding to each of\nthe detectors in the sensor. It adds a preamble,\nline start code, line length code, and other\ndata to each channel, and outputs the digital\ndata on parallel lines for recording on a tape\nrecorder. An A-scope provides the capability\nto monitor one of the output channels after\ndemultiplexing.\n\nG.3.2 Command Data\nNormally all commands are generated in the\nOCC and relayed to the spacecraft from one\nof the three prime stations. These commands\nmay be real-time commands executed in\xc2\xad\nmediately upon receipt, or time-tagged corn\xc2\xad\nmands that are stored for execution later at a\nprescribed time. In emergency situations com\xc2\xad\nmands may be sent from other stations.-Com\xc2\xad\nmands from USB stations are transmitted on\na subcarrier of the USB link and from VHF\ntype stations on the VHF link\n\nG.3 \t TELEMETRY, TRACKING AND\nCOMMAND DATA HANDLING\n\nG.3.3\n\nThe spacecraft telemetry, tracking and cornmand equipment operates with either the\nUSB or VHF type stations. The stations and\nthe NASA ground communication facilities\nprovide the link for the transfer of these data\nbetween the spacecraft and the Operations\nControl Center at GSFC.\n\nG.3.1\n\nTracking Data\n\nPrimary tracking data are obtained using the\nUSB range/range rate system. Tracking data\nare processed at the ground stations to deter\xc2\xad\nmine range, velocity and direction parameters.\nThese are then transmitted by teletype to\nGSFC where the orbital parameters and space\xc2\xad\ncraft ephemeris are computed.\n\nTelemetry Data\nSecondary tracking can be provided by the\nVHF minitrack interferometer tracking sys\xc2\xad\ntem located at VHF stations.\n\nThe spacecraft transmits real-time telemetry\ndata at 1 kbps, using the VHF downlink to\nVHF stations or a subcarrier of the USB down\xc2\xad\nlink to USB stations. These data are received\nwhenever the spacecraft is in view of one of\nthe three prime ground stations, and are\ndirectly relayed to the OCC.\n\nG.3.4 DCS Data\nData from individual Data Collection Plat\xc2\xad\nforms are transmitted up to the spacecraft at\nUHF where they are received, frequency\ntranslated, and retransmitted over the\'USB\ndownlink to one of the three prime stations.\nSpecial DCS receiving site equipment at these\nstations decodes and processes the data as they\nare received and reformats them for transmis\xc2\xad\nsion to the OCC. (Refer to Appendix E for a\nmore complete discussion of the Data Collec\xc2\xad\ntion System\n\nThe spacecraft also continuously records telemetry data on one of two on-board narrowband" tape recorders These data are played\nback at 24 kbps using another of the USB\nsubcarriers (or VHF backup donwlink) These\ndata are normally received during station contacts at Alaska or NTTF and are transferred\nin real time to the OCC. Stored data provide\na continuous history of the spacecraft and\nsensor status.\n\nG-4\n\n\nAPPENDIX H \n\nIMAGE PROCESSING FACILITY \n\nAND \n\nFILM AND DEVELOPER\n\nCHARACTERISTICS \n\n\n* Quality Control Subsystem\n* Data Services Laboratory\n* Support Services\nFigure H-1 illustrates the flow of image and\nDCS data through the IPF, and their relation\xc2\xad\nship to the various subsystems.\n\nH.1 IMAGE PROCESSING FACILITY\nThe Initial Image Generation Subsystem (I IGS)\nprocesses all image data received at the IPF\nData are accepted in the form of video tapes\nand converted to film imagery using an electron\nbeam recorder (EBR). Image annotation tapes\nprovide descriptive and positional data.\n\nThe Image Processing Facility (IPF) processes\nand stores all sensor data and disseminates\nlarge quantities of these data to distributing\nagencies in the form of film imagery, computer\ncompatible tapes, and Data Collection System\n(DCS) cards, and listings. To accomplish these\nfunctions, the IPF utilizes the following subsystems\n\n-Latent images from the IIGS are developed and\ntransferred to the Photographic Processing\nSubsystem where multiple copies are made\nfor distribution to Federal distributing agencies\nand a limited number of special project users\nThe wideband video tapes are screened for\ndata quality and user selected scenes are con\xc2\xad\nverted to high density tapes (HDTs) within\nthe Multispectral Scanner Preprocessor (MPP).\n\nSInitial Image Generation Subsystem\n(IIGS)\n* Multispectral Scanner Preprocessor (MPP)\n* General Purpose Image Processor (GPIP)\n* Digital Subsystem (DS)\n* Photographic Processing Subsystem (PPS)\nEPHEMERIS\nTAPES\n\nNASA BREIT\nDETERMINATION \n\n\nLANDSAT PROJEC\n\nOFICE\n\nEOIEMENTS\nINTERFACE\n\n\nDATA SERVICES LAB (SIGMA 5)\n\nPROCESSING\n\nIAEANTTO\n\n\nIMAGE \n\n\nTAPES\n\nTAPES\n\n\nTELEMETRY\nTAPES \n\n\nANNOTATION\n\nPROCESSING\n\nVIDEO\nTAPES\n\nMS$\nPREPROCESSOR\n\nINITIAL\nIMAGE\nGENERAT[ON\n\nHIGH \n\nITY \n\nTAPES \n\n\n-\n\nF \n\n\nDIGITAL\nSUBSYSTEM\nTAPES\n\n\nLATENTE\nHIHENBACKUPT \n\nTP(AK~\n\nCOMPUTER\nCOMPTIBLE\n\nI\n\n\nSUBSYSTEMCESSING\n7rLATENT\n\nPHTGNAPH[C\n\nFigure H-1. Production Support Services determines and controls the\nprocess flow throughout the system by means\nof work orders The IIGS also provides a backup capability to the MSS Preprocessor in\nwriting image data on HDTs.\n\nThe corrections that are applied to the data\nduring this operation include\ne \t Geometric correction for spacecraft platj\nform instabilities (Note- Digital tapes\nare not geometrically corrected )\n* \t Reduction of systematic errors caused\nby RBV camera distortion and image\ngeneration\n* \t Radiometric correction for each RBV\nand MSS spectral band\n* \t Framing of MSS data to be spatially co\xc2\xad\nincident with RBV data\n\nProcessing of DCS information in the IPF consists of editing and storing the data on magnetic\ntape, and reformatting them into computer\ncards and listings for distribution to investiga\xc2\xad\ntors. The high-resolution EBR is used\nto expose the images on 70mm film. These corrections are derived from\nmeasurements of actual RBV imagery and\nfrom preflight calibration data stored in the\nGeneral Purpose Image Processor. They cor-\n\nv-\n\n+\n22km\nObn)\nIDENTICAL\nCOVERAGE\nN\nSCENE\n\n.......\xc2\xad\n-\n\n+\\\n+\n\nrect for systematic RBV camera non-Iinearities,/\nalignment errors, and shading errors, as well as\n\n180k.\n-99,m4\n\nEBR internal errors. The correction coefficients are transferred to the IGS on computer-readable tape, stored in the control com\xc2\xad\n\nk\n\nl-km(Onm\n\nNs\n\nIDENrCAL\nCOVERAGE\nNTSIE\nPECEN\nIGMS\n\nputer, and used to control EBR writing beam\n\n-\n\nposition and intensity during image generation\nH.1.1.3 Framing\n\nFigure H-6 RBV and MSS Overlap Area Between\nConsecutive Scenes\n\nFraming of the RBV images is inherent in the\nsimultaneous shuttering action of the three\ncameras; the centers of the RB3V images are\nnominally identical. There are, however, slight\noffsets between the image centers due to misalignments between the cameras During IIGS\nprocessing, the correction data developed within the General Purpose Image Processor (GPIP)\nare used to adjust for these alignment differences and to position each image within\nthe writing area so that the latitude and\nlongitude identified in the annotation block\nrepresent the format center Format center is\nthe intersection of the diagonals between the\nfour registration marks,\n\nFor determining temporal changes in ground\nscenes, the RBV frames are scheduled in order\nto overlay, as closely as possible, previous\nframes of the same area This pattern is\nreferenced by a frame centered on the equator\n(Ref. Figure F-6 in Appendix F). The equato\xc2\xad\nrial frame is centered to within \xc2\xb1 2 seconds\nand all other frames, in that orbit, are refer\xc2\xad\nenced either forward or backward from this\nframe at 25-second intervals.\nThe MSS, on the other hand, is a continuous\nscanning device; images are constructed by\n"cutting" this continuous record into 25\xc2\xad\nsecond pieces corresponding to the RBV\nframes. In this technique, the spacecraft time\ncode of the exposure for the RBV shutters is\nused as the reference time to compute the\nMSS frame centers. Using this time and\n"counting back" the number of scan lines\nequivalent to one-half a frame, the MSS\nimagery is framed to correspond to RBV\nimagery. Framing is coincident to with-n 10\n\nDuring the sensor ON periods, RBV shutter\naction occurs every 25 seconds In this interval,\nthe satellite ground track and sensor "coverage area" advance approximately 163 kilometers (88 nm). (These values are nominal,\ndepending on orbit parameters.) This results\nin approximately 22 kilometers (12 nm) of\noverlap at the top and bottom of each RBV\nimage as illustrated in Figure H-6A.\n\nH-4\n\n\nmilliseconds of actual time of occurrence of\nthe midpoint RBV exposure. When the RBV\nis not being used, the MSS frame centers are\nstill determined from a frame centered on the\nequator with other frames spaced at multiples\nof 25 seconds from it.\n\nThe MPP and the Digital Subsystem combined\nas a processing system are referred to as the\nDigital Image Preprocessing System (DPPS).\nAn overall system configuration of the DPPS\nis shown in Figure H-9.\n\nImagery overlap between MSS frames is also\nprovided as shown in Figure H-6B. The over\xc2\xad\nlap corresponds to approximately 17 kilometers (9 nm) on the ground and is made possible by writing MSS scan lines twice - once\non each of two adjacent frames as illustrated\nin Figure H-7. The MSS overlap is limited by\nthe beam deflection aperture of the EBR.\n\nH.1.3 General Purpose Image Processor\nA key feature of the GPIP is the use of ground\ncontrol points to measure positional errors in\nthe MSS and RBV images. The ground control\npoints used for precision location of imagery\nare objects having a known position on the\nearth\'s surface that can be identified in an\nimage Data obtained from measurements\nof these control points are used in the IIGS\nfor systematic error removal.\n\nH.1.2 Multispectral Scanner Preprocessor\nThe Multispectral Scanner Preprocessor (MPP),\nshown in Figure H-8, performs a quality\nscreening function of MSS wideband video\ntapes (WBVTs) Other functions performed\ninclude inputting scenes from WBVTs, re-\n\nThe GPI P consists of aviewer/scanner assembly\n\nformatting, and recording selected scenes on\nhigh density tape (HDT). Radiometriccalibration data are extracted and recorded on the\nHDT but not applied to the data. Since neither\ngeometric nor radiometric corrections are\napplied during the process, the basic function\nperformed by the MPP is the reformatting of\nraw telemetry data onto high density tape in a\nformat compatible for processing in the Digital\nSubsystem\n\nputer Figure H-10 shows the major hardware\ncomponents of the GPIP.\n\nthat reives the 70 mm film input from the\nIIGS and associated control/interface circuits\nthat tie the viewer/scanner to the control corn-\n\nThe image measurement functions are per\xc2\xad\nformed using the viewer/scanner This instru\xc2\xad\nment is basically a precision, two-stage image\ncomparator with automatic and manual image\xc2\xad\nmatching and coordinate-measuring capability.\nThe operator station includes a data entry\ndevice, binocular viewing optics, X-Y hand\xc2\xad\nwheels and a video monitor.\nELECTRON\nGUN\n\nAPERTURE\n\n\nFIL\n\nFILM-TRAVEL\nOVERLAP AREA\n\n\nFigure H-7. GeneralPurpose\n\nH+-7\n\n\nEBR IMAGE\nCORRECTION\nTAPES\n\n\nThe computer operates as a real time control\ncomputer for image measurement and EBR\nimage correction tape generation. The computer, with associated magnetic tape unit and\nrapid access disc memory, also functions as a\ndata processor for other system operations\n\nThe high density tape (HDT) and associated\nHDTR are essential to the efficient flow of\ndata within the IPF. The HDTR provides high\nbit packing density and transfer rates during\nprocessing, along with playback at lower speeds\nto retain compatibility with the average re\xc2\xad\ncording rate on the DS computer compatible\ntapes.\n\nFigure H-11 shows the major internal functions\nof the GPIP and their relation to the system\ninputs and outputs. The normal throughput\npath is from IIGS processed RBV and/or MSS\nimage inputs to EBR image correction tape\noutput.\n\nThe control computer is used in a process\xc2\xad\ncontrol mode whereby the digital image data\nare transferred to the memory modules and the\nmain frame computer performs the data cor\xc2\xad\nrection computation. As shown in Figure H-13,\nthe subsystem reads data into the control\ncomputer memory, accepts manual instruction\ninputs, operates on the stored data to correct,\nedit, reformat, and annotatethem, and records\nthe processed data onto CCTs\n\nH.1.4 Digital Subsystem\nThe Digital Subsystem (DS) provides transformation of selected digitized data to a computer compatible tape (CCT) format. IATs are\ngenerated for the Initial Image Generation\nSubsystem and Digital Subsystem.\n\nH.1.7.1 DCS Processing\nDCS data transmissions are relayed \t by land\nlines from receiving stations to the Operations\nControl Center where the data are recorded\non the Data Collection System Tape (DCST)\nThe DCST is forwarded to the IPF where the\ndata are edited, reformatted and stored on\ndisc for up to 48 hours. At the end of each\n24-hour period, the preceding 24 hours of\ncollected data are placed on a permanent\narchive tape This transfer allows for retrieval\nof data at any time in the future, while permit\xc2\xad\nting quick access to current (the most recent\n24-48 hours) data.\n\nH.1.8 User Support Services\nThe IPF information system provides computer\nsupport for the User Support Services section\nof the IPF. Product Flow Through PPS\n\nH-- 16\n\n16MM\nBLACK\n&WNITE\nTRANSPARENCY\n\n(i.e.. band 1 annotation printed through a\nblue filter appears yellow in a color positive).\n\nimages of a scene are precision punched to\ninsure registration and then printed using appropriate color filters as the individual spectral\nimages are exposed. Both the RBV and MSS\nsensors have responses beyond the visible\nregion into the infrared. In order to reproduce\ninfrared on film it is assigned a visible color,\nusually red. Therefore, the band which is\nnominally designated as red is reproduced as\ngreen and the nominally green band is reproduced as blue.\n\nSixteen mm microfilm transparencies are made\nfrom a single spectral band of each scene of\nRBV and MSS imagery. The 70mm black and\nwhite negatives for bands 2 (RBV) and 5\n(MSS) are used as inputs to the microfilm\ncamera. For Landsat-C, it is planned to use\nband 5 for daytime coverage and band 8 for\nnighttime coverage.\n\nTable H-4.
